KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Summary and Conclusions
i.        During the decade since Independence in 1963, Kenya was successful
in expanding its important agricultural sector by an average real growth
rate of over 4% annually. While a significant part of this growth was
attributable to private large scale farming, substantial progress was also
achieved by smallholders. The latter, however, mainly focussed on export cash
crops such as tea, coffee and pyrethrum, which are grown in the better agricul-
tural areas and which could be relatively easily promoted by single crop
statutory boards. More recently, Kenya has recognized the need to give higher
priority to the needs of less privileged small farmers in other areas, which
have so far been largely excluded from the development process. Kenya thus
now aims to both widen the social impact of development and the production
base to strengthen the economy now suffering from a number of adverse inter-
national economic factors.
ii.       To meet these objectives, the Kenya Government has prepared a
new program of which this Project would constitute a first phase. Important
features of the Integrated Agricultural Development Program would be the
increased emphasis to be placed on decentralized planning and project
implementation and its aim to progressively develop comprehensive farm systems
rather than the promotion of a single crop or farming activity. In both
respects this Project would represent a significant change in the approach to
smallholder agriculture.
iii.      Government direct services to agriculture are delivered through
the Ministries of Agriculture, Cooperative Development, Natural Resources
and Works, as well as through more than 15 statutory corporations and boards.
Apart from the installation and maintenance of roads, water supplies, and
other agricultural infra-structure, these services include the provision
and regulation of research, extension and staff and farmer training, input
and output marketing, credit, price controls for inputs and outputs, and
regulation of land title.
iv.       Actual locations 1/ and detailed coverage of each Project would be
determined by detailed Work Plans to be prepared at least once a year and
agreed between Government and the Bank. Nevertheless, the design of each
phase would proceed by pre-selecting not only the provinces to be involved,
but also the districts to be reached within those provinces, with some pre-
liminary indication of locations likely to be included within those districts.
1/   The smallest adminstrative unit of the Kenya Government is the sub-
location, several of which make up a location.



- ii -
v.        Based on the criteria of past exclusion from projects, potential for
development, and availability of necessary infrastructure and institutions, 80
locations scattered through 14 districts in the Central, Eastern, Nyanza and
Western Provinces have been identified for this Project, subject to approval
through Work Plans. They contain about 283,000 holdings measuring some 1.9
million ha and cultivated by about 375,000 families, of which approximatelv
20% are expected to participate in this Project. Although the Project area
covers a wide variety of soil and rainfall regimes, most of the preselected
locations fall into either of two broad ecological zones, viz., a high rainy
zone, where coffee is the predominant cash crop, and a low (1300 m or less)
zone with generally less rainfalL where cotton is the main traditional cash
crop. Typical farm sizes are 2.5 ha in the high zone and about 3.4 ha in the
low zone. Farmers in the high zone grow, maize, beans, potatoes, and passion
fruit, in addition to coffee, while low zone farmers grow maize, beans, sun-
flower and groundnuts in addition to cotton. Most farmers who can afford to
buy and keep a dairy cow do so.
vi.       The Project would, over 5 years, benefit 70,000 farmers culti-
vating 56,000 holdings, and about equally divided both between high and low
zones and between East and West of the Rift Valley. The Project would pro-
vide a Program Unit, staff and support services for extension, input supply,
storage, marketing, animal health and husbandry and credit institutions,
as well as physical farm inputs and hired labour (both on credit), and Project
monitoring and evaluation, staff training, technical advice and studies. Not
later than December 1, 1976, and at least not later than each subsequent
September 1, Government would submit to the Bank a Work Plan, the form and
content of which are described in the Schedule to this report and were agreed
at negotiations. Except in respect of the first year's expenditures, approval
of a Work Plan by the Bank would be a condition of disbursement against
Project expenditure undertaken in an area or for a period to which the Work
Plan relates.
vii.      The Project would cost KSh 288 million (US$35.7 million) of which
34% represents foreign exchange requirements. This cost includes a 10%
physical contingency on fixed assets and a price contingency of 21% on total
base costs but excludes all duties and taxes except taxes on personal income
and fuel which are not significant. The Project would be financed by an
IDA credit of US$10 million and a Bank loan of US$10 million (which, together,
would finance foreign exchange costs plus 33% of local costs), a loan of US$5
from the Arab Bank for African Development (BADEA), a Kenya Government contri-
bution of US$6 million, and a farmers' contribution (in hired labour costs)
of US$4.7 million.
viii.     Orders exceeding US$100,000 for fertilizers (US$3.3 million), vehi-
cles, machinery and equipment (US$1 million) would be procured by interna-
tional competitive bidding in accordance with Bank/IDA guidelines. Orders
would be bulked whenever possible.- Civil works are scattered and too small
(average cost is US$8,000) to attract international bidding. Civil works
and local building materials (US$3.5 million), as well as items costing not
more than US$100,000, would therefore be procured on the basis of competi-
tive bidding advertised locally. Miscellaneous items estimated to cost



-iii -
less than US$25,000 equivalent would be purchased following appropriate
competitive procedures. There are sufficient domestic suppliers to ensure
adequate competition.
ix.       The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) would have overall responsibility
for implementing the Project. However, aspects of the Project involving
the Cooperative Bank, the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives (KNFC)
and Cooperative Unions and Societies would be carried out by the Ministry
of Cooperative Development. To ensure coordination of Project activities
at the highest level, an Interministerial Coordinating Committee would be
established under the chairmanship of the Permanent Secretary of MOA, and
with representatives from all major Project institutions. Farm inputs would
be procured and supplied to farmers by KNFC or private stockists. Credit
funds for farm inputs would be channelled mainly through the Cooperative
Bank (about 75%) but also through the Agricultural Finance Corporation
(AFC). Project output, to be delivered by farmers to their cooperatives
in the first instance, would be marketed by the established national market-
ing boards, e.g., Maize and Produce Board for all food crops, the Cotton Lint
and Seed Marketing Board for cotton, Kenya Cooperative Creameries for milk,
and Kenya Meat Commission for meat.
x.        The Project would result in incremental production of 81,000 tons
of maize, 6,000 tons of beans, 36,000 tons of potatoes, 56,000 tons of pas-
sion fruit, 2,000 tons of cotton, 6,000 tons of groundnuts, 7,000 tons of
beef and 34 million litres of milk. Sufficient demand is anticipated for
all this output. The income of a typical high zone farmer is expected to
increase from KSh 1200 to KSh 1900, and that of a low zone farmer from KSh 760
to KSh 1100 because of the Project.
xi.       The internal economic rate of return (IER) of the Project would
be 37% over a 20-year period. A 10% reduction of incremental output would
reduce the IER to 18%. With the Kenya poverty income level in 1975 esti-
mated at US$78 per capita, and with Project farmers earning the equivalent of
US$15 to US$24 per capita without the Project, the target beneficiaries are
well below the poverty level, and would have their income raised by 40% to 55%
as a result of their participation in the Project. The Project would be the
first major effort in Kenya by the Bank to benefit subsistence farmers and
strengthen institutions catering for producers of food crops. It would help
Kenya's balance of payments by reducing food imports, and if successful, could
be repeated for the benefit of subsistence farmers in other parts of the
country.
xii.      Subject to the necessary assurances, the Project would be suitable
for an IDA credit of US$10 million and a "Third Window" Bank loan of US$10
million.






KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
1. INTRODUCTION
1.01      During the decade since Independence in 1963, Kenya was successful
in expanding  its important agricultural sector by an average real growth rate
of over 4% annually. While a significant part of this growth was attributable
to private large scale farming, substantial progress was also achieved by
smallholders. The latter, however, mainly focussed on export cash crops
such as tea, coffee and pyrethrum, which are grown in the better agrictultural
areas and which could be relatively easily promoted by single crop statutory
boards. More recently, Kenya has recognized the need to give higher priority
to the needs of less privileged small farmers in other areas, which have so
far been largely excluded from the development process. Kenya thus now aims
to both widen the social impact of development and the Droduction base to
strengthen the econom.y now suffering from a number of adverse international
economic factors.
1.02      To meet these objectives, the Kenya Government has prepared a new
program of which this Project would constitute a first phase. Important
features of the Integrated Agricultural Development Program would be the
increased emphasis to be placed on decentralized planning and project im-
plementation and its aim to progressivelv develop total farm systems rather
than the promotion of a single crop or farming activity. In both respects
this Project would represent a significant change in the approach to small-
holder agriculture.
1.03      Since 1960, the Bank has extended eleven loans and credits total-
ling some US$71 million to assist Kenya's agriculture in four main areas of
activity: settlement and farm credit (,hree projects), livestock development
(two projects), afforestation for pulp and saw wood production (two projects)
and three projects in support of the development of smallholder tea ind tea
factories. Early in 1975 a loan and credit each for US$7.5 million was
extended to assist the rehabilitation of group farms. In general, projects
have been well executed although there have been substantial delays affecting
the livestock projects. A Bank agricultural sector survey identified this and
other projects.
1.04      The Project was prepared bv P'overnment with assistance fron the
Regional Mission in East Africa (RMEA). This report is based on trie findings
of an IDA mission which visited Kenya in June-July 1975 consisting of Mr. U.
Mbanefo, Mr. K. Challa and Mrs. UI. Lele (IDA) and Mr. H. Manning (Corsultant).
A further mission in September 1975 consisting of Mr. U. tlbanefo (IDA) and
Messrs. C. Spencer and B. Wickstrom (Consultants) reviewed the situation
and needs of the cotton industrv.



IT.  BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Lying astride the equator on the eastern coast of Africa, Kenva
has a total area of 583,000 km  with wide variations in climatic an:! ecoL-
gical conditions. More than half of the total area is semi-desert, biut r:e
remaining land, varying in conditions from tropical to temperate, in-'udes
some of the most fertile soils in the Continent.  The climate, modifi' bv
altitude ranging from sea level to over 5,000 meters, is primarily diFferen-
tiated in terms of rainfall regimes which vary from less than 250 mm per year
in the Northern deserts to 1,900 mm per year in the Western Highlands uv,-r
Lake Victoria.  Annual mean maximum temperature exceeds 26 0C over 7) per:ent
of the country, while the corresponding mean minimum temperature i. ah:-t 14 C.
2.02      Kenya's population is estimated at 12.9 million (mid 197t) ar-
is growing at the rate of about 3.3% annually. About 90%0 of the popul3t o,
lives in rural areas and is dependent on agriculture for its livelihood. The
average annual per capita income of those living in rural areas is probatly
around KSh 322 (US$40), well below the countrywide average of KSh 1,57"1
(US$195).
B. The Agricultural Sector
2.03      During the period 1964-73, Kenya's GDP at market prices was esti-
mated to have had a real growth rate of 6.3% annually. In the same period
the monetary sector in agriculture, accounting for less than half the value
added in agriculture, has been growing at 4.9% annually, and the non-monetary
sector at 3.8%, giving a combined growth rate of 4.3% a year.  This growth
rate was less than in other sectors (e.g., industry and mining at 8.5") and
as a consequence the percentage contribution of agriculture to GDP nas been
declining from around 40% in the mid-sixties to about 30% today. Nonetheless
agriculture accounts for over 60% cf total exports of which coffee and tea
are the most important. Other agricultural exports include pyrethrum (701  of
world production), sisal, wattle extract, cotton, nuts, fruits, vegetabLes,
meat, wool, dairy products and animal feeds.
2.04      If one defines large farms as 20 ha and above, there are about 1.2
million smallholdings in Kenya, of which about 50% are less than 2 ha, and
occupy less than 4% of the total cultivable land. Most smallholdings are
farmed by their owners, many of them women, and include squatters on (>vern-
ment land or estates, residents of "group owned" latge farms, and "acceptees"
who farm the land of neighboring tribes to which they do not belonig. About
20% are progressive commercial farmers, another 20% are in a transitional
stage between subsistence and commercial production and the balance of some
60% are subsistence farmers.  The most pr,sperous smallholders' income is
probably several times that of the subsistenre farmer, many of whom extract
a meager and precarious livelihood under uinfavorable agricultural conditions.



-3-
2.05      In addition to maize which is the staple in most of the country,
and sorghum and finger millet widely consumed in the low-lying areas, small-
holders also produce beans, coffee, tea, pyrethrum, cotton, oilseeds, live-
stock, milk, potatoes and fruits. Many smallholders belong to one or more of
the 1,500 active cooperative societies which not only process and market
coffee, pyrethrum, cotton and dairy produce, but also supply farm inputs,
short-term credit, cattle dipping facilities and tractor services.
C. Services to Smallholder Agriculture
2.06      Government services to agriculture are delivered through four
ministries and 15 statutory production boards. The Ministry of Agriculture
(MOA) has the primary responsibility for agricultural development and farmer
services. The Ministry of Cooperative Development (MCD) supervises coopera-
tive organizations, and provides them with management advice. The Ministry
of Works and the Ministry of Water Development provide and supervise crop
extraction roads and rural water supplies respectively. The statutory boards
involved in agriculture are either general, as the Maize and Produce Board
which trades in most food crops, or crop-specific, e.g. the Kenya Tea Develop-
ment Authority, the Coffee Board and the Pyrethrum Board. Many of these
institutions enjoy monopoly powers and each plays an important role in its
particular field. The initiation and implementation of development projects
have been decentralised through the increased use of the District Development
Committee which discusses and monitors all development projects at the dis-
trict level. The District Commissioner acts as Chairman of the Committee,
whose membership includes local chiefs and farmers' representatives in addi-
tion to the District Agricultural Officer and other Government officials.
2.07      Research and Extension Services.  Agricultural research has in
the past been mainly crop oriented and has not specifically addressed the
needs of smallholders. However, much of this research, notably in the dev-
elopment of new maize varieties, is adaptable to the needs of small farmers,
and some appropriate input packages have been established. Although Kenya has
a large extension service for both crops and livestock, its effectiveness
is impared in three ways: first, many of its staff have inadequate technical
background requiring intensified in-service training; second, much of its
better staff has been over specialized and lack an adequate understanding
of total farm needs; and third, the services suffer from inadequate recur-
rent budgetary support which seriously affects staff mobility and effective-
ness.
2.08      Training of staff and farmers is provided for by a large network
of colleges and training institutions. Senior agricultural staff have usually
been trained to university degree level within the University of Nairobi
or overseas. Intermediate level staff are trained to diploma standard at
the Egerton, Embu and Bukura agricultural colleges while junior staff obtain
their training either at Embu, Bukura, or it Farmer Training Centers. Most
districts have at least one Farmer Trainin- Center (FTC), designed to intro-
duce farmers to modern techniques through short residential courses. The FTC



-4-
intake includes both male and female farmers. It is important to ensure that
women are adequately represented in the intake, since they look after many of
the farms. Because of budgetarv constraints a large number of FTCs have
been closed for long periods each year which has considerably reduced their
effectiveness.  MOA in 1972, initiated a training scheme designed to update
the training of all extension officers, particularly in the technological
aspects of crop and animal production. In the same year the Ministry created
the Land and Farm Management Division which has given special emphasis to
specialist staff improving farm management standards.
2.09      Supply of fertilizers, pesticides, and seeds has been undertaken
in the past mainly by private firms and organizations, the largest single
distributor being the Kenya Farmers Association (KFA) through a national
network of branches and stockists. Government is now the sole importer of
all fertilizers and while practically all artificial fertilizers have to be
imported, the Kenya Seed Company (KSC), a commercial organization, undertakes
the multiplication of maize, sunflower and pasture seed. MOA seed inspection
service certifies that seed offered for sale meets legally prescribed purity
and germination standards. Planting materials for tea, coffee, and pyrethrum
are supplied by the respective development agencies through nurseries located
in main production areas. Cotton seed is supplied by the Cotton Lint and
Seed Marketing Board through the ginnery network.
2.10      Credit.  Institutions providing agriculture credit reflect the
historical pattern of development in the sector with the needs of large
farms and estates being provided by commercial banks, the larger commercial
small farmers by the well-established Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC)
and with other smallholder credit being limited to the promotion of specific
crops through the cash crop agencies such as the Kenya Tea Development Autho-
rity the Pyrethrum Marketing Board, the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Board
and the Horticultural Crops Development Authority. Outside these crop autho-
rities there has in the past been little institutional smallholder credit and
farmers have either borrowed from local traders or through other informal
credit transactions at the village level. In recent years, however, Kenya
has given active encouragement to the development of the cooperative movement
and an increasing volume of credit is now channelled from the Cooperative
Bank, established in 1968, to unions, primary societies and the Kenya National
Federation of Cooperatives, in particular for the development of coffee.
Interest rates to farmers which are subject to Government approval, can range
between 9 and 12%, although, in practice short-term interest has been kept
down to 10% or 11%.
2.11      Marketing of smallholder produce has traditionally been undertaken
by traders, stockists and cooperatives. Some specific crops (mainly for
export) e.g., coffee, tea, cotton, as well as meat and milk are marketed
through statutory monopolies. The Maize and Produce Board (MPB) which ope-
rates through a network of agents, primarily markets maize, but also buys
many other crops (especially pulses and oilseeds) either as an agent for
processors or for direct export.



2.12      Prices of a wide range oF agricultural produce are fixed by Govern-
ment under the Price Control Act of 1971. Thus, prices are set by Government
and implemented by parastatal organizations at producer, processor, and
retail levels for maize, wheat, rice, sugar, milk, cattle, cotton (except
retail) and wattle bark. Government is in the process of establishing a
regular price review mechanism in MOA.
2.13      Individual (transferable) freehold title to land applies in most
parts of Kenya. Government is committed to a program of land adjudication
and registration designed to provide farmers, wnerever possible, with
transferable title to their land. This process has either been completed
or is underway in each district in the Project Area.
111. THE INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
A. Program Concept, Objectives and Strategy
3.01      In its endeavour to both broaden its agricultural production base
and bring about a more equitable distribution of development to the rural
areas, the Kenya Government is introducing its Integrated Agricultural
Development Program which it intends should provide the future major thrust
of its assistance to small farmers throughout the country. Compared with
past efforts in this sector, the Program would have two important innova-
tive features.
(i) It would seek to promote a "whole farm" 1/ approach to
development in contrast to the single crop aproach which
has characterized past efforts. While this has come to
be accepted as the central objective in its own right,
its adoption is made necessary by the absence of any
predominant crop or farming activity in many areas which
have hitherto been left out of the development process.
Indeed past neglect in many areas has, to a considerable
extent, stemmed from the lack of a single development
opportunity, and the lack of mechanisms to handle the
multi-activity approach;
(ii)  It would encourage the development of project planning and
implementation responsibilities at the local (administrative
District) level. Although tentative steps have been taken
1/   Refers to an approach which addresses all the productive activities
on the farm rather than just one crop or one activity.



- 6 -
in this direction in the past (notably under the earlier
Special Rural Development Program 1/) effective local plan-
ning involvement has only recently begun to emerge.
The Program would thus seek to improve small farm management in the broadest
sense through a strategy of phased cevelopment based on criteria which take
into account not only potential for increasing crop and livestock yields
but also the extent to which areas have hitherto been left out of past de-
velopment efforts.
3.02      The consequences of the orientation changes - from the 'vertical'
to the 'horizontal' development approach are two-fold. First, although
the potential effects in the long-term are likely to be far reaching, the
approach must essentially be evolutionary. In the short term, the Program
cannot fully address all the constraints and needs arising out of the diversity
of activities envisaged. In particular technical innovations and packages
have not yet been fully developed for all crops which may be promoted and the
composition of the district development committees (responsible for local
planning and implementation) and their relationship with the provincial author-
ities, the Central Program Unit and specialistiGovernment departments will
need to evolve in the light of experience. Furthermore with the exception of
the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Board, which would play a key role in view
of cotton's importance as an anchor crop, the Program cannot fully address
initially some of the weaknesses in organizations such as the Kenya Cooperative
Creameries (KCC) or the Kenya Meat Commission (KMC) which would be involved
in the Program to a less significant degree than the CLSMB. Furthermore, the
need for a Program of this kind is too urgent to await complete resolution of
the slightly overlapping roles of the Cooperative Bank and AFC in supplying
credit to smallholders (a problem which the Kenya Government is currently
discussing with the Bank). However the orientation of the Program is in
itself seen as a means of bringing about such needed improvements over time.
3.03      Second, the Program's implementation would need to be especially
flexible so as to respond to previous experience and reflect this in annual
work plans. The phasing and the pattern of farming activities are therefore
expected to vary in some respects from the models used in planning the Project.
3.04      Initially at least, the Program would not provide for investments
in rural roads, potable water and other economic and social infrastructure
for two reasons. First, there are considerable areas where adequate infra-
structure already exists to support effective production activities including
areas which hitherto have been agriculturally neglected. Second, investments
1/   The SRDP started in 1971 attempted to address a variety of rural needs
e.g. agricultural research, maize production, livestock marketing in six
administrative divisions. For further details see Uma Lele, The Design
of Rural Development.



in other sectors (rural roads, water and education) either already are, or are
soon expected to be supported by other Bank project assistance,and are designed
in a flexible way which will permit close linkages with the further stages
of the Program, and the required coordination would be facilitated by its
encouragement of local planning capabilities.  Overall it is the Program's aim
to achieve a balanced growth between production and infrastructure support to
assure an acceptable economic return to the country and at a cost to the
Government which can be sustained over the long-term.
B. Program Planning and Implementation
3.05      The lowest administrative unit is the sub-location, several of
which form a location; locations make up a division, several of which form
a district; several districts form a province. There are seven provinces
in Kenya, all of which would be ultimately brought within the Program through
a phased development. The Program would be administered along the same
geographical pattern as other Government services. Thus the district would be
the basic level at which most Program activities would be planned and executed
after approval at the national level. Overall executive responsibility
for Program implementation at the district level would be vested in the
District Agricultural Officer (DAO), as is the case with most agricultural
development efforts in Kenya. However, inter-ministerial and inter-institu-
tional coordination of Program plans and activities would be undertaken by the
District Coordinators. District work plans would be approved by a Committee
consisting of the DAO as chairman, the District Cooperative Officer, the
District Program Coordinator as secretary, the relevant cooperative union
manager, the District Development Officer and the AFC Branch Manager. Further
details of administrative and management arrangements are in Chapter V.
C. The Project Area (Map and Annex I)
3.06      The approval of a location to participate in Project activities
would be governed by the principles and criteria described in Chapter IV and
the Schedule to this report. Based on criteria such as the extent of past
exclusion from projects, potential for development, availability of the nec-
essary infrastructure and availability of institutions capable of making an
early contribution to development, a preliminary identification of 80 loca-
tions scattered through 14 districts in Eastern, Central, Nyanza and Western
Provinces has been made. Inclusion of a location in the preselected 80 does
not guarantee that location's participation in the Project, nor does exclu-
sion from the 80 necessarily preclude any location in the Project districts
from being proposed in Work Plans. The preselected locations contain some
283,000 smallholdings cultivated by about 375,000 families (in some instances
2 or more family units cultivate one holding), of which only 56,000 holdings
cultivated by about 70,000 families (20%) are expected to be reached bv the
Prcject. Other families and holdings in the 80 locations, and the other



- 8-
locations in the 4 provinces would come within the scope of subsequent proj-
ects. The following table illustrates the broad Project objective:
Total in
Preselected Locations               Project Objectives
Holdings  Area (ha)  Families   Holdings  Area (ha)  Families
East of Rift
(Eastern and   147,000   1,342,000   194,000    28,000    157,000    35,000

The 372,000 ha Project area would at full development consist of 81,000 ha
uinder crops, 84,000 ha under pasture, and the rest firewood areas, rocky out-
crops and homesteads.
3.07      The Project area includes a wide variety of soil and rainfall
regimes but, for all practical purposes, the pre selected locations fall
into either of two broad ecological zones, viz a high zone where coffee
is the main cash crop, with maize and beans as the main food crops, and a
low zone where cotton is now the predominant cash crop, but which is also
suitable for cultivation of sunflower, groundnuts, maize, sorghum, millet
beans and peas. Both zones are found in each of the four provinces which
would be involved in the Project; sometimes, as in Machakos, they are found in
the same district. Nevertheless, their features, and the development problems
they present are quite different. The high zone has higher rainfall, denser
population and smaller holdings. Furthermore its farmers have upgraded their
cattle to a greater extent (80% of total grade cattle in Project) have bene-
fitted more from highland farm technology, and have more advanced farm insti-
tutions e.g. the coffee cooperatives.
3.08      The low zone (1300 m or less) has less reliable precipitation during
each of the two rainy seasons. The need to produce a given quantity of food
in essentially less favorable conditions than in the highlands calls for
larger size farms, as well as the spreading of the crop risk through sequential
planting of each of the crops, and the avoidance by and large of perennial
crops. Most of the country's rain-fed cotton is grown in these areas. They
also account for 80% of the Zebu type cattle in the Project area, although
here, too, smallholders have been trying to upgrade their cattle stock in
recent vears through cross-breeding using artificial insemination.



-9-
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
4.01      The Project, which would constitute part of a new long-term program
for the development of smallholder agriculture, would, over 5 years, promote
the establishment of "whole farm" systems through the provision of inputs
and the strengthening of extension services and institutions to meet the
needs and support required for a wide range of crop and livestock activities
within 14 districts of four of Kenya's provinces. While a precise definition
of its scope and activities would be the subject of Work Plans to be agreed,
at least annually, the Project is expected to encourage the production of
maize, beans, cotton, potatoes, passion fruit, oil seeds, meat and milk
by about 70,000 small farmers operating some 56,000 holdings.  Specifically
the Project would provide for:
(a) the establishment of a Program Unit,
(b) staff and support services for extension,
(c) cooperative staffing for input supply,
(d) staff and storage facilities for marketing,
(e) staff and support for credit institutions,
(f) physical input requirements,
(g) on-farm hired labor,
(h) staff and facilities for livestock production and health,
(i) Project evaluation,
(j)  staff training, tecinical advice and studies.
The basic planning and management unit would be the administrative district
and the Project would be under the overall direction of the new unit in
MOA.
B. Project Phasing and Physical Development
4.02      Not later than December 1, 1976, and not later than September 1 in
each subsequent year during the Project period, Government would submit to the
Bank a Work Plan. The submission of Work Plans and the criteria and guide-
lines on which they would be based as set out in the attached Schedule were
agreed during negotiations, and approval of a Work Plan by the Bank would be a



- 10 -
condition of disbursement against Project expenditure undertaken during the
period to which it relates, except for .he first year s (1976/77) Project
expenditure estimated at US$0.6 million which would be disbursable without a
Work Plan but would be included seperately, with the 1977/78 costs, in the
first Work Plan to be submitted to the Bank by December 1, 1976. The date
fixed for the submission of the Work Plans assumes a crop and fiscal year from
April 1 through March 31. However, the short rains (October to December) are
important for some crops in certain areas, especially in Western and Nyanza
Provinces. Government may therefore submit a subsidiary Work Plan in March
for Project inputs and planting the following October, in respect of those
areas and crops which would benefit from Project activities during the short
rains.
4.03      Project development is expected to be phased as follows:

The above projected phasing is an indicative guideline for planning purposes,
as actual participation would depend on the aggregate Work Plans agreed
between Government and the Bank. Together with pre-Project preparatory work,
the first year (1976/77) would be spent in appointing new staff, the prepara-
tion and approval of the annual Work Plan, selecting the first participating
farmers, and ordering inputs. The first Project planting season would be
March/April 1977.
4.04      The Project aims to reach the same number of farmers in the dis-
tricts East of the Rift Valley as wculd be reached West of Rift. Project
farmers would be about equally divided between farmers living in the high
zone cultivating maize, beans, potatoes, passion fruit and livestock and
those living in the low zone and who, in addition to maize, beans and live-
stock, would cultivate groundnuts and cotton which are suited to the hotter
and drier zone (para. 3.07). Although some crops not listed above (e.g.
sunflower) could be included in a Work Plan, and although such Plans would
govern the actual rate of crop and livestock development, annual expansion
of Project activities is expected tc proceed as follows:

C.  Detailed Features
Program Unit (Annex 2)
4.05      The Project would provide for vehicles, supporting services and
staffing for a new Program Unit in the Land and Farm Management Division of
the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA). The unit would be staffed in Nairobi
by a Head of Program, a Deputy Head of Program, a Financial Controller,
an Evaluation Officer (para. 4.13), a Farm Management Officer, and one Project
Liaison Officer in each of the five main divisions of MOA. Field Staff
of the Program Unit would consist of 4 provincial and 14 district Project
Coordinators to provide coordination at the field level of Project activities
of the MOA, MCD, AFC, MPB and other Project institutions. Government has
identified an experienced civil servant as a possible Head of Program; and
assurances were obtained at negotiations that his appointment, together with
those of the Financial Controller and Evaluation Officer would be made, after
consultation with the Bank, not later than August 1, 1976. Provincial and
District Coordinators would be appointed not later than June 1 preceding the
initial participation of their areas in Project activities, except in the
first year, when the relevant date would be September 1 (i.e. September 1,
1976, for 1977/78 participation).
Extension and Training (Annex 3)
4.06      The Project would provide  for incremental vehicles, housing and
support facilities required for the extension services. Provision is also
made for the capital costs of setting up and equipping demonstration farms
in Farmer Training Centers (FTCs) and for the incremental costs of reorient-
ation courses in these centers for extension staff and new Project farmers.
To ensure adequate representation of women in FTC intake (para. 2.08),
assurances were obtained during negotiations that Government would use its
best efforts to involve more women both in the training and extension services.



12 -
One of the problems encountered in increasing the effectiveness of extension
staff has been the failure to provide enough incentives to agricultural and
veterinary staff. In a civil service structure which provides uniform terms
of employment to staff at the same grade across sectoral boundaries, it is
difficult to provide for special treatment of employees in one sector. None-
theless, the Bank discussed with Government at negotiations circumstances
under which better incentives could be offered, and Government undertook
to forward to the Bank, not later than June 30, 1977, criteria for evaluating
performance of extension staff and a scheme to provide appropriate incentives.
Input Supply (Annex 4)
4.07      While some private traders would continue to sell inputs in markets
within the Project area, most Project physical farm inputs would be procured
by the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives (KNFC) which would distri-
bute inputs to cooperative unions and through them to societies and farmers.
The Project would provide KNFC with two 14-ton trucks, a central store in
Nairobi containing a plant for packaging seed and fertilizer in 10 kg bags
for smallscale farmers, two regional stores (1 East and 1 West of Rift),
housing, and 2 personnel vehicles. The Project would also provide for three
incremental senior and ten junior- staff for KNFC as well as a new senior
officer in MOA to coordinate the entire national supply of inputs between
importers and distributors.
Storage and Marketing Facilities (Annex 5)
4.08      The Maize and Produce Board (MPB), the national monopoly buyer
of most food crops surplus to subsistence needs, would be strengthened
through the provision of 10 additional field officers, inspectors and
supporting staff, 3 produce collection trucks, two-4500 ton central crop
stores, a 10-ton per hour crop drier, and operating costs. The Horticultu-
ral Crops Development Authority (HCDA) which markets potatoes would also be
strengthened through the provision of 3 stores. The Project would also pro-
vide materials for construction by MPB of two stores per district. Assur-
ances were obtained during negotiations that MPB would appoint all participat-
ing cooperative societies and unions as agents for the purchase of all mem-
bers' food crops. Assurances were also obtained from Government that Project
cooperative societies and unions would be appointed sole buyers of seed cotton
and that each society and union would only buy seed cotton from unregistered
farmers after it has first made reasonable efforts to confirm that such
farmers were not indebted to another society.
Smallholder Credit Institutions (Annex 6)
4.09      The Project would provide for vehicles and office equipment as
well as incremental staff for credit institutions as follows: 1 Head Office
and 4 field officers for the Cooperative Bank, 4 Head Office and 20 field
officers for AFC, 2 Head Office and 14 district officers for the Ministry
of Cooperative Development which supervises all cooperative activities, and,



- 1 3 -
on an annually decreasing cost basis, 4 Cooperative Union Managers and 10
Cooperative Society Managers for the few Project unions and societies which
initially cannot pay for these staff out of their own income. The latter
support would gradually reduce during the Project period as these coopera-
tive institutions become financially self-sufficient.
Physical Farm Inputs (Annex 6)
4.10      The Project would provide for the supplv of credit to farmers on
a revolving fund basis for annual physical inputs as follows:

On-Farm Hired Labour-(Annex 6)
4.11      One of the most severe development constraints in many parts of the
Project area is the lack of adequate funds for hiring labour to meet demand
peaks, i.e., during land preparation. Therefore an incremental cost of on-farm
hired labour of about KSh 74 million (US$9 million) has been estimated in the
Project (Annex 10, Table 12), and it is proposed to offer farmers credit for up
to 75% of their on-farm requirements including hired labor.  25% of the approved
credit would be provided in cash and the rest in kind.
Livestock Production and Health (Annex 8)
4.12      The Animal Production Division and the Veterinary Department of MOA
are responsible for Government efforts in livestock production and animal
health respectively. The Project would provide for an artificial insemination
(AT) laboratory and office, crushers, veterinary offices and stores, cattle
dips, milk handling and cooling buildings, housing vehicles and equipment, as
well as salaries of 10 livestock field officers and junior field staff, and
incremental vehicle operating costs of the Veterinary and Animal Production
Departments in the Project area. At present, dip management standards vary
from area to area, and artificial insemination has been introduced in some



- 14 -
areas without adequate health measures. Accordingly, the Work Plan would
provide for management of communal cattle dips by the Veterinary Department
and provision of dips, acaricide and hand pumps as a precondition for expan-
sion of the artificial insemination scheme in the Project area.
Project Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 9)
4.13      For the purpose of monitoring and assessing detailed progress, the
Project would provide for the employment of an Evaluation Officer and 15
enumerators, as well as vehicles, equipment and support services. The Project
Evaluation Officer and enumerators would be part of the Program Unit in the
Land and Farm Management Division of MOA. A statistical survey expert to
be hired by August 1, 1976, would establish the initial methodology for data
collection and analysis. Two different types of evaluation (micro and macro-
evaluation) would be undertaken respectively in two divisions of MOA. Firstly,
there would be a detailed monitoring and micro-evaluation of Project progress
to be undertaken by the Evaluation Officer and his staff, and reported in each
Project Quarterly Report. The monitoring indeces here would consist of the
number of participating farm holdings, farm sizes, cropping patterns, crop and
livestock yields and output, number of participating farmers and farmers'
incomes. Secondly, there would be a continuing macro-evaluation, the findings
of which would be included in the third and subsequent Work Plans. 1/ In this
way a close connection would be established between evaluation and planning
throughout the life of the Project. This macro-evaluation would be undertaken
either by the Evaluation Section in the Economic Planning Division (EPD) of
the MOA, which is now being expanded, or by the Central Bureau of Statistics
(CBS). EPD/CBS evaluation indices would include the effectiveness of Project
management and institutions, types of Project beneficiaries, employment, income
distribution, aggregate production, foreign exchange costs and benefits,
budgetary implications and other sectoral and macro-economic aspects of the
Project. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the monitoring and
evaluation programs would be undertaken as indicated.
Training
4.14      Government is currently discussing with external aid donors new
proposals for the training of extension staff. Government has satisfactory
training schemes for most other grades of Project staff, but this needs to be
supplemented by more management oriented training such as is provided by the
Management Training School in Swaziland which would provide appropriate train-
ing for senior Project staff. The Project would therefore provide KSh 2
million (US$250,000) to be spent on Bank approved staff training and fellow-
ship schemes.
1/   The required timing of the second Work Plan would be such that
insufficient evaluation data would be available from the first
Project year activities.



- 15 -
Technical Advice and Studies
4.15      The Project would provide KSh 6 million (US$750,000) for Bank
approved technical advice, studies and consultancies. This provision would
cover ad hoc studies directed at specific Project needs and in addition the
proposal to provide periodic in-depth technical advice from an international
agricultural institution (possibly research oriented) was discussed during
negotiations. It was agreed that Government would include specific proposals
for technical assistance and studies in the Work Plans.

Costs have been estimated at prices expected to prevail as of February 1976
and include capital costs plus incremental operating costs directly attribut-
able to Project activities. A physical contingency of 10% has been included
on civil works, motor vehicles, and equipment. A price contingency equal to
21'  of base costs was also included, calculated at the following rates:  civil
works at 14'% in 1976, 12% in 1977 to 1979 and 10% in 1980; motor vehicles and
equipment at 10' in 1976 and 8%ZO thereafter; farm inputs, salaries and other
operating costs 10%' a vear. Except for tax on salaries and fuel which is not
si-nificant, Project costs do not include any taxes or duties.
4.17      Credit totalling KSh 112 million (US$14.0 million) would be provided
under the Project on a revolving and incremental basis (Annex 10, Tables 11,
12 and 13) for fertilizers, insecticides, seed and hired labor. In accordance
with national cooperative credit regulations in force as of March 31, 1976,
the credit would be limited to 75% of the estimated amount of these on-farm
costs, and would be provided in kind, save for 25% of the approved credit
(i.e. KSh 28 million or US$3.5 million) which would be provided in cash. In
order to meet the needs of farmers who cannot provide the level of security cur-
rentlv demanded under existing cooperative and AFC regulations, a Subsistence
Farmers Credit Fund (SFCF) of KSh 4.7 million (US$0.6 million) would be set
aside out of the above-mentioned fund for on-farm credit, to be loaned to
subsistence farmers through cooperative institutions. Further details of
this scheme and other credit terms are discussed in paragraphs 5.05 to 5.07.

the terms of which are discussed in paragraph 5.07, and a loan agreement be-
tween Government and BADEA would be a condition of loan/credit effectiveness.
F. Procurement
4.19      Orders exceeding US$100,000 for fertilizers (US$3.3 million),
vehicles, machinery and equipment (US$1 million) would be procured by inter-
national competitive bidding in accordance with Bank/IDA guidelines. Orders
would be bulked whenever possible. Civil works are scattered and too small
(average cost is US$8,000) to attract international bidding. Civil works
and local building materials (US$3.5 million), as well as items costing not
more than US$100,000, would therefore be procured on the basis of competitive
bidding advertised locally. Miscellaneous items estimated to cost less than
US$25,000 ecuivalent would be purchased following appropriate competitive
procedures. There are sufficient domestic suppliers to ensure adequate
competition.
C. Disbursement (Annex 11)
4.20      Disbursements of funds from both the Bank loan and IDA credit
would be on the following basis:
(a) 100% of foreign expenditures or 85% of total expenditures on vehi-
cles, machinery and equipment; fertiLizers; technical assistance
and studies;
(b) 85% of total expenditures on civil works;
(c) 80% of loans disbursed to farmers in Project areas (i.e. apart
from fertilizers); and
(d) 65% of total expenditures on fuel and oil.
All items are detailed and individually costed in ,nnex 10. Di;h;Ursements
against civil works, vehicles, machinery, equipment and fertiLizers would
be fully documented.  Disbursements against loans to farmers would be             ->

ment against all other costs would be made against certificates of expenditure
l/   The Cereals and Sugar Finance Corporation (CSFC) is a small corporation
within the Treasury which is used by Kenya Government :i.-ost exclusively
for the purpose of channelling funds to other institutions (e.g. AFC)
which execute the projects or farm credit components tuiereot.

endorsed by the Head of Program, the documentation for which would not be

at the end of the Project may be reallocated with Bank approval.
H. Accounts and Audit
4.21      The Financial Controller in the Program Unit would establish and
maintain adequate books and records to accurately reflect the assets and
liabilities of the Project and the receipts and payments in respect of the
Project, in such a way as to enable identification of such assets, liabili-
ties, receipts and payments pertaining to the Project and not to other
activities of MOA. Similarly, the MCD, MPB, Cooperative Bank, AFC, KNFC,
the Cooperative Unions, and Societies involved in the Project, and HCDA, being
prospective beneficiaries under this Project, would maintain books and records
sufficient to facilitate identification of all assets, liabilities, receipts
and payments pertaining to the Project. Assurances were obtained during nego-
tiations that the accounting records described above would be established and
maintained, and that the accounts of all the organizations and institutions
mentioned above would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to the
Bank.
4.22      Quarterly Reports of the Project activities, achievements, and
problems would be prepared by the Head of Program (para 4.05). Assurances
were obtained at negotiations that the Project Quarterly Reports would
be forwarded to the Bank promptly, and that the annual accounts of the Minis-
tries and institutions referred to above and CLSMB would be audited by inde-
pendent auditors acceptable to the Bank and would be forwarded to the Bank not
later than six months following the year to which they relate. Assurances
were also obtained during negotiations that a suitably qualified internation-
ally recruited Project Financial Controller would be appointed after consulta-
tion with the Bank.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A.  Project Direction and Coordination
5.01      The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) would have overall responsibility
for implementing the Project. However, under this overall direction, those
aspects of the Project involving the Cooperative Bank, the Kenya National
Federation of Cooperatives, and Cooperative Unions and Societies would be
carried out by the Ministry of Cooperative Development. To ensure proper
coordination at the highest level, Government and the Bank agreed during nego-
tiations on the establishment of an Interministerial Coordinating Committee



- 19 -
under the chairmanship of the Permanent Secretary of MOA, and with the Director
of Agriculture as a member. The Committee would meet from time to time at the
request of the Head of Program and in any case at least once a quarter to
approve Work Plans, review Project progress, approve the Project-Quarterly
Report (para 4.22) and to resolve problems. The Ministry of Cooperative
Development would be represented on the Committee by its Permanent Secretary
and by the Commissioner of Cooperatives; the Ministry of Finance, the AFC, the
MPB, the Cooperative Bank, the CLSMB, the KNFC and HCDA would each be repre-
sented by the Permanent Secretary or General Manager of the institution. The
interministerial Committee would be established prior to September 30, 1976.
5.02      At field level the district is the basic management unit and,
a committee consisting of the DAO, the District Project Coordinator, the
District Cooperative Officer, the District Development Officer, the Coopera-
tive Union Manager and the AFC Branch Manager, would approve district Work
Plans, review progress, and receive monthly and quarterly Project reports from
the District Project Coordinator. Provincial Commissioners, District Comis-
sioners, Chiefs and Sub-Chiefs would be closely associated with the Project
and would be encouraged to advise Project staff, and to employ their consider-
able local influence in the furtherance of the Project objectives.
B. Detailed Organization (Annex 2 and Chart)
5.03      Within MOA, overall Project direction would be the responsibility
of the new Program Unit consisting of the Head of Program, Deputy Head of
Program, Financial Controller, and Evaluation Officer and their supporting
staff. Extension and training, livestock development, and animal health
services would be provided by the regular services and staff of the re-
spective divisions of MOA. The supply of inputs would be organized by the
Program Unit, with the advice of the extension staff. The normal channel
for most farm inputs on credit would be from KNFC to the cooperative unions,
and from these to cooperative societies for distribution among their members.
However, to a small extent, some farmers may buy inputs from private stockists
for cash, or on credit from AFC. Farmers would store small quantities of
produce for short periods, but the main responsibility for storage of produce
would rest with the cooperative societies and unions, the Maize and Produce
Board (MPB) and the Horticultural Crops Development Authority (HCDA). Market-
ing of produce would be mainly by cooperative societies acting as agents for
MPB, HCDA, the KCC, the Kenya Meat Commission (KMC), the CLSMB and the Coffee
Board (KCB). These statutory institutions are the ultimate sellers and
exporters of farmers' produce. Credit for farm inputs would be channelled
from the Treasury's CSFC through either the Cooperative Bank and unions or to
a lesser extent through AFC. Project monitoring and evaluation would be
shared between the new Evaluation Officer and either the Central Bureau of
Statistics or the Economic Planning Division of MOA (para 4.13). The Ministry
of Cooperative Development (MCD) would continue to exercise its supervision
of the management of the Cooperative Bank, KNFC, Cooperative Unions and
societies involved in the Project. Evidence of continued good management of
these institutions would be required in Work Plans (schedule and para 4.02).



- 20 -
5.04      The Project would thus involve a large number of Kenyan institu-
tions which are already rendering services to farmers. Most of them are
functioning with moderate success, and a substantial part of Project costs
would be incurred in strengthening the key Project institutions with a view to
improving their effectiveness. The KMC, the KCC, and the CLSMB, all national
institutions with responsibility for some Project marketing, suffer at pre-
sent from financial or management problems. As the Project's dependence on
KIC and KCC would be relatively small, and as their situations do not at the
moment threaten loss of Project benefits, no measures are now proposed for
their strengthening but their positions would be kept under review. The
CLSMB, (Annex 7) however has suffered from a series of management and finan-
cial problems dating back several years. Cotton is not only the traditional
cash crop in about half of the area (the low zone), but it is also sold
through a monopoly marketing organization (CLSMB), and would therefore serve
as an 'anchor' crop providing some security for lending institutions.
Efficient management of CLSMB is therefore of considerable importance for
achieving Project objectives. Government agreed with the Bank during negotia-
tions on certain proposals for strengthening CLSMB and improving CLSMB's
methods of operation. The filling of Board vacancies and hiring after consul-
tations with the Bank, of suitably qualified persons as Financial Controller,
Marketing Officer and Chief Accountant are conditions of loan/credit effec-
tiveness.
5.05      Traditionally, credit schemes have tended to by-pass some farmers
because of their difficulty in providing adequate security. Yet such farmers
lack the resources to buy modern inputs for cash, and, without credit would
be unable to increase their output sufficiently to provide the necessary
security. In a pilot effort to break this vicious circle KSh 4.7 million
or US$0.6 million sufficient for about 3,500 farmers would be set aside exclu-
sively for such farmers in a Subsistence Farmers Credit Fund (SFCF) to be
administered by the Cooperative Bank through cooperative institutions. The
results of this pilot scheme would be carefully monitored during the Project,
and if successful could be expanded through later projects. Criteria for
selection of participants and procedures which would be similar to those of
the Cooperative Production Credit Scheme and the similar ongoing USAID Project
for Subsistence farmers (detailed in Annex 5), were agreed during negotiations.
5.06      Most of the lending to small scale Kenyan farmers is through co-
operative societies, which also mobilize farmers' savings for smallholder
development. The main national institution is the Cooperative Bank of Kenya
Limited, which lends to Cooperative Unions to finance their members' agri-
cultural and marketing operations. Most of the Project financing for on-farm
inputs would therefore be channeled from the Treasury through its Cereals and
Sugar Finance Corporation (CSFC) the Cooperative Bank and thence to the
Cooperative unions and societies to finance farmers' purchases of inputs. Some
farmers have traditionally been customers of AFC, and credit sufficient for
their on-farm inputs would be channeled from the Treasury through CSFC to
AFC. The estimated credit amounts to be channeled through cooperatives and
AFC would be stated in each Work Plan: present estimates indicate that 75%
(including SFCF) would be channeled through the cooperatives and 25% through
AFC. Both the Cooperative Bank and AFC are currently operating satisfactorily.



- 21 -
5.07      Apart from SFCF financing, Project funds for cooperative farm
credit would be advanced to CSFC at 5% for 20 years (including 5 years of
grace). CSFC would onlend such funds to the Cooperative Bank for 24 months at
5-1/2%. Each annual loan to AFC or the Cooperative Bank (to be made up of the
annual requirements for farmers credit) would be repaybale in full on maturity
in 24 months. The Cooperative Bank would on-lend to unions at 7% annually and
these in turn to societies at 9% (in each case loans would be for 22 months).
The cooperative societies would lend to farmers at 11% for periods not exceed-
ing 18 months. For SFCF (para 5.05) Government would on-lend to CSFC at 3%,
and CSFC to the Cooperative Bank at 3-1/2%, the Cooperative Bank would lend to
unions at 5%, unions to societies at 7% and societies to farmers at 10%.
Lending periods would be the same as for regular cooperative credit operations.
Funds would be channeled from CSFC to AFC for 24 months at an interest rate of
5-1/2% for on-lening to farmers at 11% for up to 18 months. Farmers would
be able to borrow up to 75% of their annual requirements of fertilizer seed
and other on-farm expenses. Only 25% of the approved credit would be advanced
in cash; the rest would be in kind. These and other loan terms and conditions
(detailed in Annex 5) were agreed during negotiations.
Staffing

Detailed lists of incremental staff posts are in Annex 10.  Job descriptions
for the four senior Project positions (Head of Programi, Deputy Head of Program,
Financial Controller and Project Evaluation Officer) are in Annex 2. It
is anticipated that the Head of Program would be a Kenyan, but that staff
for the other three top positions would be recruited internationally. In
that event, Kenyan counterpart staff would be recruited and the necessary
training provided (para 4.14). No difficulty is envisaged in recruiting the
rest of the staff set out above. They consist mainly of 500 dip attendants
(MOA) and other junior employees who are readily available.



- 22 -
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, FARMERS' BENEFITS AND EFFECTS
ON GOVERNDENT BUDGETS
A. Yields and Production
Yields
6.01      Yields are particularly difficult to forecast in this Project be-
cause development in each district will vary according to the District Work
Plan, and because of the wide variety of geographical areas and institutions
encompassed by the Project. Yields would be generally greater for farmers
in the higher (usually rainier) zone than for those in the low zone. Yield
estimates are averages taking into account the likelihood of drought in one
year out of four. Yields would thus be higher than these averages in good
years and lower in bad. For the typical farmer, yields are expected to in-
crease cumulatively until reaching the projected maxima in the fourth harvest
following the farmer's first introduction to Project services. For the typi-
cal high altitude farmer cultivating 2.5 ha, yields are expected to improve
over four years as follows:  maize from 1 ton/ha to 2.7 ton/ha; beans from
0.54 ton/ha to 1.1 ton/ha; potatoes from 5 ton/ha to 18 ton/ha; passion fruit
from 5 ton/ha to 15 ton/ha; milk from 1,100 litres to 1,700 litres per cow,
and beef from 100 to 130 kg/ha. For the lowland farmer typically cultivating
about 3.4 ha yields are expected to increase over four years as follows:
maize from 0.8 ton/ha to 2.3 ton/ha; beans from 0.54 ton/ha to 1.1 ton/ha;
cotton from 0.23 ton/ha to 0.65 ton/ha, groundnuts (in shell) from 0.60 ton/
ha to 1.2 ton/ha; milk from 1,000 litres to 1,600 litres per cow, and beef
from 100 to 130 kg/ha. Smallholders in Kenya have, under similar conditions,
with average levels of husbandry, obtained yields comparable to the above
maxima. The four-year build up period assumed is to enable farmers to become
accustomed to the Project's innovations and improved farming systems.
Production
6.02      On the basis of the above yields, Project activities at full de-
velopment (year 8, 1983/84) would result in the following incremental produc-
tion: 81,000 tons of maize valued (at 1983/84 prices in 1975 constant terms)
at KSh 51 million, 6,000 tons of beans worth KSh 9 million, 36,000 tons of
potatoes worth K.Sh 12 million, 56,000 tons of passion fruit valued at KSh 26
million, 2,000 tons of cotton worth K.Sh 4 million, 6,000 tons of groundnuts
worth KSh 9 million, and 7,000 tons of beef worth KSh 21 million and 34 mil-
lion litres of milk worth KSh 23 million. Inevitable changes in price rela-
tionships are likely to produce changes in the quantities of production
indicated above, and could lead to production of crops not at present
anticipated.



- 23 -
B. Markets and Prices (Annex 13)
Markets
6.03      Sufficient demand is anticipated for all Project output.  With the
exception of some passion fruit juice, higher quality cotton, and some beef,
the bulk of the Project output would be consumed within the country. There
exist well-established marketing channels, which would be further strength-
ened through this Project by provision of more staff, vehicles, equipment
and storage. Most Project farmers are expected to sell their cash and sur-
plus food crops to their Cooperative Society in the first instance, but the
Maize and Produce Board (MPB) would ultimately handle Project farmers' sur-
pluses of maize, beans, and groundnuts, practically all of which would be for
domestic consumption, mainly in the urban areas. The proposed agreement to
permit cooperative societies to purchase maize and other food crops for which
MPB is responsible (para 4.08) would result in a more complete coverage of
farmers, would alleviate local shortages and surpluses and thereby stabilize
prices. Cooperatives would sell processed cotton lint to the Cotton Lint
and Seed Marketing Board (CLSMB) (para 5.04). Cooperatives would also mar-
ket passion fruit (part of which would be processed into drinks at existing
plants) and potatoes (through the Horticultural Crop Development Authority
as well as traditional marketing channels); the milk would be sold to Kenya
Cooperative Creameries, and beef to the Kenya Meat Commission.
Prices
6.04      Prices of all Project output are fixed by parastatal organizations
and approved by Government, at both farmgate and consumer levels. Farmgate
prices currently paid for proposed Project crops are generally adequate to
provide enough incentive for farmers to use modern inputs, and farmers are
usually able to sell small amounts above the official prices when the latter
fail to reflect true scarcities. There is, however, an increasing awareness
of two factors in this connection: firstly, absolute prices should not be
fixed in isolation without, at the same time, determining relative prices,
between, for example, beef, maize and cotton which compete for factors of
production and are clearly substitutes in the farmer's planning. Secondly,
much more information is required on a continuing basis than is available
concerning markets, supply and demand factors, and substitutability and com-
plimentarity in both production and consumption, in order to determine appro-
priate commodity prices. Kenya Government has expressed its intention to
undertake a regular review of agricultural commodity prices. Assurances
were, therefore, obtained during negotiations that Government would set up
a unit within MOA to formulate recommendations on absolute and relative
agricultural prices, and would review annually the farmgate prices of meat,
milk, maize, seed cotton, groundnuts, beans, sunflower seed, and passion
fruit, and publish the prices simultaneously-by December 15 each year.



- 24 -
C. Farmers' Benefits (Annex 14)
6.05      The Project would provide intensive extension service and modern
farm inputs on credit to about 70,000 farmers most of whom have not previous-
ly benefitted from such institutional assistance. Farmers' benefits would
vary considerably because of the wide range of conditions included in the
Project areas. The annual income of a typical low zone farmer cultivating
about 3.4 ha is expected to increase from about KSh 760 before the Project
to about KSh 1,100 at his fourth harvest and thereafter. Within the same
period the annual income of a typical high zone farmer cultivating 2.5 ha
would increase from about K.Sh 1,200 to about KSh 1,900. Although the
Project would call for large investments of materials (mainly fertilizer,
seed and insecticide) and readily available labor, those farmers prepared to
undertake such investments would gain worth-while returns.
D. Effects on Government Budget (Annex 15)
6.06      As this Project would benefit lower income rural families it would
result in direct budgetary transfers from higher income taxpayers to rural
small scale farmers who pay only indirect taxes. There are no direct means
whereby the Government can recover from beneficiaries the full cost of the
proposed Project services and investments, and specific charges for Project
services would be contrary to Government policy. Government revenue from
excise and purchase taxes would of course increase as Project farmers would
spend their increased incomes. However, the only direct source of Govern-
ment income from the Project is the margin between the interest payment to
the Bank Group and the interest Government would charge the Cooperative Bank
or AFC. Government would, therefore, ostensibly suffer an annuai deficit of
about K.Sh 20 million (US$2.5 million) as a result of staff, fuel and other
operating costs. Government is aware of the implications of this Project,
and that the recurrent costs would be higher with subsequent phases. Assur-
ances were obtained during negotiations that Government would review on a
regular basis the recurrent budget implications of this and other projects
in the agricultural sector so as to ensure that they can be sustained over
the periods for which they are designed.
VII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
(Annex 16)
7.01      The primary direct benefits of the Project would be increased
production of maize, beans, potatoes, passion fruit, cotton, groundnuts,
milk and beef.
7.02      The Project's internal economic rate of return (IER) is estimated
to be 37% over a 20-year period. All Project costs, except the cost of



- 25 -
training, research and studies were included in the calculation. The costs
of farm production inputs and value of Project incremental output are based
on recent World Bank projections. The Project would not result in any incre-
mental use of family labour. It would, however, result in a considerably
increased use of hired labour during peak periods (270 man-days a year instead
of 96 man-days a year for the average Project farm). The hiring of labor in
the Project area is an established tradition and sufficient casual labor is
available to meet Project demands. Hired labor has been fully costed at
market rates. Foreign exchange costs and benefits are valued at KSh 9.30 =
US$1.00 to reflect the economic cost of foreign exchange to Kenya; if foreign
exchange were valued at the prevailing exchange rate of KSH 8.05 = US$1.00,
the economic rate of return would be 34%.
7.03      Risks associated with rainfall and other climatic conditions have
been taken into account in estimating Project benefits (Annex 8). One impor-
tant source of risks that could affect the Project's rate of return includes
the possibility that Project institutions might fail to reach the expected
number of farmers with services and inputs. To minimize this risks Project
implementation would be governed by detailed Work Plans (Chapter IV and
Schedule); furthermore, a number of assurances affecting the operations of
Project institutions were obtained during negotiations. Another risk is that
farmers may adopt the recommended input packages less rapidly than anticipated
owing to the relatively high cost of inputs and the associated financial risk
in case of crop failure. However, the promotional efforts of intensified and
strengthened extension services are specifically designed to demonstrate the
benefits to be derived from adoption. Even if incremental output from the
Project were reduced by 10% the rate of return would still be 18%. If Project
benefits were delayed by one year throughout the Project period and Project
costs incurred as anticipated, the rate of return would be 12%.
7.04      Project benefits go beyond quantifiable increases in production.
The Project would represent the first major effort in Kenya to benefit small
farmers producing mainly food crops. It would revitalize the institutions
supplying services to these farmers and focus attention directly on their
problems. Some Project expenditures, notably the US$3.5 miLlion to be spent
on civil works, would benefit subsequent projects. Assuming successful imple-
mentation of this first phase Project, an instrument would have been forged
for reaching small-scale farmers throughout the country. These benefits are
expected to accrue without detriment to the environment.
7.05      With the IBRD calculated poverty level for Kenya at US$78 per capita,
and with prospective beneficiaries currently earning the equivalent of US$15
to US$24, including cash and subsistence, the Project is clearly addressing
the needs of the poorer segments of Kenyan society. The Project would at 1975
constant prices increase beneficiaries' incomes by 40% to 55%. Individual
benefits would be considerably larger for some 5% of the poorest Project
farmers who are currently not eligible to receive credit but would be given
credit under the Project.



- 26 -
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
8.01      The Project would be implemented in accordance with a Work Plan
(para 4.02) to be prepared by Kenya Government at least once a year, and
agreed between Government and the Bank; and except for the 1976/77 Project
costs of about US$0.6 million (para. 4.02), such agreement would be a con-
dition of disbursements for the period and area to which the Plans relate.
Details of the Work Plan which were agreed during negotiations, are in the
Schedule'to this report.
8.02      The following would be c.onditions of loan/credit effectivenes:
(a) execution of a loan agreement between Kenya Government
and BADEA;
(b) execution of an onlending agreement between the Govern-
ment and CSFC, and subsidiary loan agreements between
CSFC and AFC and between CSFC and the Cooperative Bank;
and
(c) appointment, after consulting the Bank, of a Financial Controller,
the Chief Accountant, and a Marketing Officer for CLSMB.
8.03      Following are some of the more important assurances obtained
during negotiations:
(a) Government would appoint, not later than August 1, 1976,
and after consultation with the Bank, the Head of Program,
the Program Financial Controller, the Project Evalua-
tion Officer and a statistical survey expert acceptable
to the Bank (paras 4.05 and 4.13);
(b) Government would appoint not later than September 30,
1976, the Interministerial Coordinating Committee
described in para 5.01);
(c) Government would set up a unit in MOA to review and publish
agricultural prices annually (para 6.04);
(d) Government would regularly review the recurrent budget
implications of this and other development projects in its
agricutlural sector to ensure that they can be sustained
over the periods for which they were designed (para 6.06);
and
(e) A micro-evaluation of the Project would be undertaken by
the Project Evaluation Officer and his staff, while a macro-
evaluation would be carried out by staff of either the Central
Bureau of Statistics or the Economic Planning Division of MOA;
and those evaluations would cover the matters stated in para
4.13.



- 27 -
8.04      The proposed project constitutes a suitable basis for an IDA
credit of US$10 million on standard IDA terms and a "Third Window" Bank
loan of US$10 million for 25 years including 7 years of grace.

respectively the items specified in (a) (i),

to come from the Subsistence Farmers Credit Fund

programs for each location involved.

of the Credit and the Loan proposed to be made available

described in Schedule 3 to the Development Credit
Agreement.

Part C:   Criteria on which approval of proposed Work Plans will be based and
conditions of approval
(1) Suitability of locations proposed for inclusion in the
Project, taken as a group, will be determined on the
following bases:
(a) Selection of locations having been undertaken
by the Head of Program in consultation with senior
provincial and district agricultural staff.
(b) Participating farmers from the East Project Area
will comprise not les than 40% of all participants.
(c) Participating farmers from the West Project Area
will comprise not less than 40% of all participants.
(d) There would be a progressive increase in participating
locations and farmers during the Project implementation
period.
(2) Suitability of individual locations proposed for inclusion in
the Project will be determined on the following bases:
(a) General - adequacy of infrastructure, e.g.
water, access roads, markets.
(b)  Crops   -  assured availability of processing
facilities.
(c) Livestock (if included in Work Plan) -
(i)  Management of cattLe dips by the Vaterinary
Department.
(ii)  Availability of hand sprayers or dips and
acaricide (either already supplied or to be
supplied).
(d) Cooperative Societies -
(i) All participating cooperative societies and unions
should be capable of ensuring the supply of credit,
inputs and marketing services for all crops as well
as cattle and milk.
(ii) Financial solvency.
(iii) Adequacy of management.



-5-
(e) Inputs -
(i)  Maize and cotton seed proposed to be used
should be tested and have an 80% chance of
germination.
(ii)  Fertilizer variety and proposed rate of
application should conform to locally
recommended packages.
(f) Number of Extension Staff -
(i)  Enough junior agricultural assistants (JAAs)
and technical assistants to ensure 2 hours of
instruction per Project farmer per month, through
individual visits and group meetings but, in any
event, enough to achieve a ratio of not less than
one of the same for each 200 farmers in the
location involved.
(ii) Technical Officer for the division in place.
(g) Training -
(i)  All participating JAAs and technical assistants
should have received orientation training for
the Project, or would be scheduled for such
training during period covered by proposed Work
Plan.
(ii)  All farmers participating in the Project for the
first time should have received Project orienta-
tion training or would receive such training
during such period.
(h) Credit Sources -
(i)  The main channel of credit for farm inputs
would be the cooperative societies and unions.
(ii)  The Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC) would
make seasonal loans only where no suitable co-
operative lending channel exists or where
participating farmers are already AFC clients or
are applying to AFC for medium or long term loans
at the same time.
(iii)  Participation in the Subsistence Farmers' Credit
Fund (SFCF) would be reserved for farmers who do
not qualify for conventional Cooperative or AFC
loans.
