(c) Ordering of Farm Inputs: Provision would be made
for ordering farm inputs not later than six months
before anticipated date of use (e.g., November 1
for March/April planting).
(4) Proposed Work Plans will not be considered in the absence
of the approvals indicated below:
(a) District Work Plans shall be submitted to the Head
of Program through the provincial Director of Agri-
culture by the District Agricultural Officer (DAO)
who shall, by signing the District Work Plan,
certify that it has been considered in full committee
and approved by a committee consisting of the DAO,
as chairman, District Cooperative Officer, District
Program Coordinator as secretary, cooperative
union manager, District Development Officer and AFC
branch manager.
(b) The proposed Work Plan shall be submitted to the
Bank by the Head of Program, and shall be signed
by the chairman of the IADP Interministerial Co-
ordinating Committee who shall in this capacity
act as a representative of the Kenya Government,
and who shall, by so signing, certify that the
proposed Work Plan has been considered in full
committee and approved by such committee.

KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Physical and Demographic Details of Project Areas
General Characteristics
1.        The Project area covers almost the whole range of geographic charac-
teristics existing in Kenya. It encompasses 80 locations in 22 divisions,
involving 14 districts in Central, Eastern, Western and Nyanza Provinces.
Eastern Zone (or East or Rift) comprises Central and Eastern Provinces. The
total area of the Eastern Zone districts included in the Project is 36459 sq.
km.  Nyanza and Western provinces comprise the Western Zone (or West of Rift),
with a total area of 20749 sq. km. Thus the total area encompasing the pro-
ject is 57,208 sq. km, which represents only 10.1 percent of the total land
area of Kenya, although it contains nearly 60 percent of the national popula-
tion (1975 estimates - see Table 2).
2.        Both of these zones are therefore among the most densely populated
rural areas in the country. Table 2 indicates that according to the 1975
estimates Kisii district has the highest population density, followed by
Kakamega, Kiambu and Kisumu. The same estimates show that Kakamega district
has the largest population followed by Machakos, South Nyanza and Kisii.
Project districts, their areas, populations, rainfall, and other physical,
economic and social features are detailed in Tables 1 through 12 of this
Annex.
3.        Topographically the locations preselected for the Project range
in alitude from 2,000 metre (Bungoma District) to 600 metres (Machakos Dis-
trict). Land types range from undulating and hilly with highly fertile, deep
loam soils (Kakamega District) to flat, plains bordering on low fertility
soils (Eastern Embu District). Soil types range from dark red friable clays
(Machakos/Kisii) to light brown sandy loam (Busia/Bungoma). In general, all of
the locations have reasonable annual rainfall (Table 3). Meru receives the
highest annual rainfall (2920 mm) and Nyeri the lowest (860 mm). The annual
temperature range between project locations is determined largely by altitude,
varying between locations within the ranges 45 - 700 F (mean minimum) to
70 - 98 F (mean maximum). A more detailed discussion of the agricultural
characteristics of the proposed project areas is in Annex 8.
Population, Occupation and Social Services
4.        Of the estimated Kenya population of 13.5 million people (1975),
7.9 million or 59 percent Live within Ihe districts which would be
included in the project (32.1 percent western zone, 26.8 percent eastern
zone - see Table 2).  The popl(Lation of the Project areas in common with



ANN EX
Page 2
the whole country, is predominantly agricultural and,of the districts con-
cerned, 5 percent (some 400,000) of the total population are in wage employ-
ment, compared with only 3.5 percent of the population as a whole (Table 4).
It is Government policy to encourage rural employment so that the flow of job
seekers to the urban areas can be reduced. Table 4 shows that casual employ-
ment is higher in the rural areas than regular employment. The majority of
the employees in the rural areas are in the agricultural sector. 1/ Seasonal
fluctuations in labour requirement in agriculture explain why casual employ-
ment is higher than the regular employment in the rural areas. On average,
wages of employed people in these areas are in line with national estimates.
5.        In general the proposed project area enjoys better social services,
e.g. education and medical facilities, compared with other areas. About 70%
of the primary schools, and about 73% of the secondary schools in Kenya are
in these provinces (Table 5). This table also shows that about 74% of
the primary school and 64% of the secondary school enrolments in Kenya in
1973 were in these four provinces. Conversely, Table 6 shows the provision
of public health services in the project districts, where 18,000 hospital beds
in Kenya (46%) are in the four provinces. The ratio of persons per hospital
bed at 988 compares badly with the national average of 686 people.
Project Coverage
6.        On the basis of a detailed survey, involving all divisions of the
Ministry of Agriculture Headquarters and field staff at all levels, the most
suitable locations were identified for participating in the Project. The
main criterion for involving specific locations was lack of extensive benefit
from previous schemes linked with relatively dense population and distinct
potential to benefit from concentrated, coordinated development. The
project would aim to involve 20 percent of all holdings in the locations
covered.
7.        Table 8 details the planned implementation of the Project in the
eastern zone (Eastern and Central provinces). Based on the surveyed data 2/
for total number of holdings size (column f) for each division,2/ to obtain
the total farm area of participants at the end of the fourth project year
(column e). Column (d) shows the surveyed 2/ total geographic area of the
locations concerned. This column deviates from direct proportion to the total
area assumed for participant holdings partly because of small variations in
holding size from the average taken in column (f), but mainly because of varia-
tion in non-farming areas, roads, forest, towns, water, rock etc., between
locations.
8.        The average holding size (column f) represents the total "shamba"
area, including cultivated land, pasture, homestead and some allowance for
domestic firewood areas, but excluding access roads, communal areas, etc.
1/     Source:  Employment, Incomes and Equality (A strategy for increasing
productive employment in Kenya (ILO, Geneva, 1972).
2/     Mlinistry of Finance and Planning Department of Statistics -
Statistical Abstract 1974.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
9.        An asessment has been made (column a) of the total number of
families included in the project area. This estimate is based on the
surveyed data 1/ from the eastern zone (giving a ratio of approximately
1.25 families per holding). This calculation is made in order to provide
an estimate of the number of families and hence, taking the average figure
of 6 members per family (as per census), in order to establish the total
number of people expected to be reached. Table 9 summarises the planned
areas and participants expected in the eastern zone at project completion
(year- 5).
10.       On a similar basis to the east, Table 10 provides the area and
numbers of holdings and people (estimated) to be involved in the Project
in the western zone (Western and Nyanza Provinces). This table is cal-
culated on an identical basis to Table 8, the only exception being column
(a), where, in the absence of census data, estimates of family numbers
have been based aproximately on the surveyed data from the east, i.e. 1.25
families per holding. Table 11 summarises the planned involvement for the
western zone in the Project. The distibution of participating holdings,
areas and families between eastern and western zones differs slightly from
the figures in Chapter III because of rounding which has had the effect of
equalizing proposed participation east and west of the Rift Valley.
1/   Ministry of Finance and Planning Department of Statistics -
Statistical Abstract 1974.

ANNEX 2
Page I
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization, Management and Staffing
Broad Responsibilities and Project Coordination
1.        The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) would have overall responsi-
bility for implementing the Project. However, those aspects of the Project
involving the Cooperative Bank, the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives
(KNFC), and Cooperative Societies and Unions would be implemented by the
Ministry of Cooperative Development (MCD). To ensure proper coordination of
this Project at the highest level, Government and the Bank agreed at negotia-
tions the establishment of an Interministerial Coordinating Committee under
the chairmanship of the Permanent Secretary of MOA. The MCD would be repre-
sented on the Committee by its Permanent Secretary and the Commissioner of
Cooperatives; the Ministry of Finance, AFC, the Maize and Produce Board (MPB),
the Cooperative Bank, KNFC, and the Horticultural Crops Development Authority
(HCDA) would each be represented by the Permanent Secretary or General Manager
of the institution.
2.        At district level the Project would come under the regular review
of the District Development Committee (chairmaned by the District Commis-
sioner) which would receive monthly and quarterly Project Reports from the
District Project Coordinator. There would also be Provincial Coordinators
to coordinate Project activities at the provincial level. As far as possible,
Provincial Commissioners, District Commisioners, Chiefs, and Sub Chiefs would
be closely associated with the Project and would be encouraged to advise
Project Staff, and to employ their considerable local influence in the
furtherance of Project objectives.
Detailed Organization (Chart)
3.        The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA), undergoes frequent reorganiza-
tions designed to enable it to cope better with the ever-changing pattern
of activities and projects (including this project) which it has to under-
take. The organization chart at the end of this report shows that Project
management (consisting of the Head of Program, Deputy Head of Program,
Financial Controller, and Evaluation Officer) and their supporting staff
(including provincial and district coordinators) would be located in the
Land and Farm Management Division of the Ministry of Agriculture.
4.        The Ministry of Cooperative Development has enough representation
at the provincial and district levels to enable close supervision of coopera-
tive activities in most areas. Indeed the Ministry frequently takes over



ANNEX 2
Page 2
the day-to-day management of ailing cooperative societies and unions. Manage-
ment standards in cooperative and other Project institutions would feature
prominently in the Work Plan (Chapter IV and Schedule).
Management and Staffing
5.        At completion in Year 5 the Project would be employing an incre-
mental staff complement totalling almost 900 and involving an annual wage bill
of about US$1 million.  Goverment is aware of this burden of recurrent cost
and is prepared to finance it on a continuing basis. The top management of
the Project Unit would consist of the Head of Program, his Deputy, the Finan-
cial Controller, and the Evaluation Officer, all to be located in the Land and
Farm Management Division of MOA. They would be supported by provincial
and district staff, and, through Project liaison officers one of whom would
be in each division of MOA, would be backed by the full range of services
supplied by that Ministry. The staffing of the MCD, AFC, KNFC, Cooperative
Bank, Cooperative Unions and Societies, MPB and HCDA would all be strengthened,
both at the Head Office and in the field, to enable them to perform their
project roles. Table 1 of this Annex details all the proposed staffing,
salary scales, and recruitment time-table. The qualifications and terms
of reference for the four most senior members of the Project Coordinating
UJnit are set out in the attached appendices.



ANNEX 2
APPENDIX 1
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Head of Program (JG 'M')
Qualifications
B.Sc Agriculture with specialization in Farm Management. Knowledge
of/and experience in agricultural, marketing, planning, and implementation
of projects will be necessary. The officer must have served in the field
for a considerable period to be able to have acquired administrative experi-
ence in financial and personnel management.
Duties and Responsibilities
This officer will be responsible to the Director of Agriculture
for all aspects of the Project implementation. He will in particular be
responsible for planning, programming, pricing, monitoring and guiding pro-
grams, preparation of Work Plans and Project Estimates, the formulation of
policy for the Program and providing technical guidance to the field extension
staff. It will also be his responsibility to coordinate other production
programs - Animal Production and Crop Production, applying research data at
farm level. Close liaison is therefore necessary with the Heads of Crop
Production Division, Animal Production Division, Economic Planning Division
and the Director of Research. He will also serve as secretary of the proposed
Program Interministerial Coordinating Committee.



ANNEX 2
APPENDIX 2
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Deputy Head of Program (JG 'L')
Qualifications
B.SC. Agriculture with specialization in Farm Management or B.Sc.
Agriculture with considerable experience in Crop and Animal Production. The
officer must have held district responsibility to be able to have acquired the
necessary financial and personnel management experience, and to have admini-
stered agricultural projects.
Duties and Responsibilities
This officer will be responsible to the Head of Program. In parti-
cular he will be responsible for:
(a) Staff training and personnel matters of the Project;
(b) Liaison with agricultural credit institutions, ministries
and departments dealing with credit, such as Department of
Settlement, Cooperative Department Finance and Planning,
Ministry of Works and Ministry of Water Resources;
(c) Organization and acquisition of farm inputs:
(d) Preparation of progress and annual reports of the Project;
(e) Project review and research;
(f) Preparation of development and recurrent estimates and
control of expenditure for the Project;
(g) Any other Project responsibilities delegated to him by
the Head of Program.



ANNEX 2
APPENDIX 3
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Financial Controller
Qualifications
A qualified accountant or a graduate in business management with
at least 5 vears experience in accounts and senior financial management.
Duties and Responsibilities
He will be responsible to the Project Coordinator for all financial
matters.  He will be in particular responsible for:
(a)  financial administration and accounting for the Project's
finances.
(b)  all purchases of Project inputs.
(c)  financial returns.
(d)  accounting and safe custody of Project stores and equipment.
(e)  preparation of estimates - both recurrent and development in
consultation with the Deputy Coordinator.
(f)  preparation of Project Quarterly Reports.



ANNEX 2
APPENDIX 4
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Evaluation Officer (JG 'L')
Qualifications
B.Sc. (Agricultural Economics) or B.Sc. Agriculture with specializa-
ti-n in f3rm -ninaaement and extension.  Also experience in analyzing and
interpreting statistical data.
Duties and Responsibilities
This officer will be responsible to the Head of Program for monitoring
and evaluating and progress of the Project. He will in particular be respon-
sible for:
(a)  Organization and collection of basic farm output/input data
in collaboration with the Farm Management Branch (FMB).
(b)  Analysis, interpretation and monitoring of farm management
data to the extension staff through the F.M.B. This is to
be on a continuous basis, should start with establishment of
base-line data before the Project, and include in parti-
cular reliable data on the number of farm families covered
by the Project, their incomes, their farm sizes, cropping
patterns, and crop, milk and beef yields.
(c)  Evaluation of the Project performance suggesting adjustment
and reorientation when and if necessary.
(d)  Farm Management Research in collaboration with the Farm
Management Research Unit of the Farm Management Branch to
build up data for planning purposes.

ANNEX 3
Page 1
KFNYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMIENT PROJECT
Extension and Farmer Training
1.        The four most important factors militating,.against the effec-
tiveness of the extension service have been:
(a)  inadequate technical background of the jun or staff, thereby
requiring considerable in-service training.
(b) many of the good extension staff may be over-specialized,
without an appreciation of total farm needs. This calls for
reorientation courses to prepare such staff for the total
farm approach of projects such as this.
(c)  there are no clear incentives to encourage extension staff
to better performance.
(d) inadequate recurrent budgetary support has seriously reduced
mobility of extension staff as they frequently ran out of funds
for fuel for their vehicles.
These problems, and what this Project would do to alleviate them, are
discussed below.
Technical Background
2.        There are still at the lower levels of the extension service,
staff recruited many years ago, when the degree of technical training required
of a junior recruit was rather lower than today. Government policy is to
retrain such staff and thus improve their effectiveness in the extension
service, rather than reassign them to other duties. In keeping with that
policy, this Project would provide for the cost of short reorientation and
re-fresher courses for 3,700 extension staff members at farmer training
centers. It is recognized however, that this training is Project-oriented
(para 3 below), and cannot replace the widespread staff training program
being undertaken by MOA for country wide junior extension staff requirements.
Specialists Versus Generalists
3.        There has been some controversy regarding the appropriate degree
of specialisation for the lowest Level extension staff. Specialisation of
farm level staff in crop production (or in one or two crops, e.g. coffee
or tea) or in animal husbandry, was, to some extent at least, a response to
the criticism that generalist staff knew so littLe about any specific farm
problem that, sometimes, the farmer was better informed than they were.
On the othier hand, such specialisation sometimes went so far that junior
staff sometimes faiLed to appreciate, and frequentLv failed to address, the



ANNEX 3
Page 2
totality of the farm problems. Following the call for specialisation, there
are now, at farm level, a large number of junior specialist extension staff.
The formation of the Land and Farm Management Division was an effort to
reorientate extension staff towards the total farm concept. This is the
main problem which would be addressed by the Project orientation courses
planned for some 3,700 junior extension staff at the Farmer Training Centers
during the Project period (para 2 above). For the future MOA plans to limit
specialisation to a relatively small number of staff to be called in to
deal wi-th specific problems in their fields of specialisation. The bulk of
the lowest level staff, however, would be generalists under the control
of the new Deputy Director of Agriculture who would also be head of the
Extension and Training Division.
Staff Incentives
4.        The provision of incentives to high performance among extension
staff poses a number of difficult practical problems. Firstly, it is impos-
sible to identify good performance unless there are acceptable criteria for
assessing performance. At present such criteria are based largely on the
observations of supervisors and on farmers' reports. Nevertheless, such
evidence is frequently inconclusive. Secondly, even if it is possible to
identify good extension staff, it is difficult to offer them any financial
incentives or any other incentives based on improved employment benefits,
since these are governed by rules applying equally to all departments of the
civil service, and administered by the Public Service Commission. However,
MOA rewards good performance with more rapid promotions, sometimes into special
posts such as those being created under this Project.
Recurrent Budget
5.        Perhaps the commonest manifestation of inadequate budgetary alloca-
tions for recurrent expenditure on agricultural extension are the frequent
complaints by extension staff that they are unable, for long periods at
a time, to operate their vehicles, because of lack of fuel. Another frequently
heard complaint is that the Farmer Training Centers are closed for long
periods during the year, because they are not provided with adequate funds to
meet the costs (food, power, teaching materials, water, etc.) of farmer
training courses. In an effort to avoid these problems in the Project area,
the Project would provide for incremental operating costs of the extension
service vehicles, as well as the costs of running reorientation courses in the
FTCs during the Project period.
Farmer Training Centers (FTCs)
6.        There is at least one FTC in each of the districts proposed for the
Project, and information on FTC facilities and programs would be contained
in the Work Plan (Chapter IV and Schedule). Briefly, the FTC consists of a
large farm on which can be found the buildings necessary to accommodate groups
of farmers selected to attend orientation courses on a range of farming or
associated courses. The teaching element is based on the assumption that
farmers can be shown the real potential of agronomic recommendations made by



ANNEX 3
Page 3
extension personnel. The other element consists of business activities of
the (often sizeable) demonstration farms which could easily be financialLy
self-supporting. There are, therefore, two contrasted functions which
unfortunately are not, at present, financially distinct. In order to
fulfill these separate functions, it would be preferable to have separate
financial provision for, on the one hand, a teaching and accommodation
budget, and on the other, an attempt at commercially oriented farm manage-
ment. Current treasury regulations demand that revenue from crop or live-
stock production in the FTC immediately be passed into the revenue account.
As a result, towards the end of the financial year when funds are becoming
low, not only does the teaching often cease, but even vital farm operations
can be prejudiced by actual cash shortage. It is therefore considered
essential to operate the farm enterprise aspect of the FTC as a normal
commercial enterprise, either with an imprest account, or with net sur-
pluses paid to Government every year after retaining sufficient working
capital.
7.        The challenge to the agricultural officer-in-charge of ensuring
real profitability of the farm operation would serve a number of important
purposes. Firstly, the extension personnel would quickly learn the extent
to which their recommendations (frequently based solely on textbook informa-
tion) were applicable in their particular environment. Secondly, the problems
of timely use of on-farm inputs would be tested under field conditions.
Even where the investment might be more than the smallholder could perhaps
afford, at least there would be incontrovertible evidence of the potential.
Thirdly, those farmers selected for the course would at least be offered
the opportunity of questioning the practicability of the proposed input
package. Fourthly, the extension personnel would in turn be able to set up
an illustrative holding for which proper costings would be kept to arrive
at the economically viable technical packages.
8.        The educational value of the emphasis on the commercially oriented
farm would not be limited to the Junior or General Assistant grade of exten-
sion personnel. Even the District Agricultural Officers could well benefit
from the experience. Moreover, the feed back from FTC to farmer, and vice
versa, would highlight problems to which the Research Division could be
directed (such as variation in plant density to meet specific ecological
regions). The illustrative typical smallholder farm to be set up within
the FTC would also be valuable in determining yield, cost and income levels
which the smallholder could be expected to achieve. The problem of ensuring
proper accounting for all Treasury (or indeed any other) funds invested in
FTC commercial farm operations can be overcome hy an Imprest Account System
wqhereby each FTC is provided with an Imprest Fund for which it accounts to
the Treasury whenever the FTC seeks replenishment of the Imprest Account.



ANNEX 4
Page 1
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Input Supply
Distribution Channels
1.        The timely supply of the appropriate fertilizers, improved seeds,
insecticides and chemical sprays, has been one of the most significant con-
straints to small farmer production in Kenya, not only in remote areas but
also in other areas where smallholder cash crops have not made much headway.
2.        Historically the production and supply (including importation of
agricultural inputs where necessary) has been operated reasonably well by
the private sector. Three or four major suppliers have imported sufficient
balanced fertilizers for the country's requirements until 1973/74, when short-
ages occurred largely because of the critical world supply situation. The
major seeds are supplied by Kenya Seed Company (KSC), a successful commercial
business largely Government owned. Pesticides and insecticides are produced
or imported as necessary by the manufacturers or their local agents.
3.        So far, these various supplies have been distributed mainly through
two channels, namely, the Kenya Farmers Association (Cooperative) Limited
(KFA) and its network of branches and agents which have the most comprehensive
coverage, and the cooperative movement (para. 6). According to information
provided by the KFA, in 1975 it dealt with 2,212 stockists, who were supplied
inputs on a cash basis. Approximately 1,600 of the total appointees were
regular operators handling large quantities of supplies.
4.        Although KFA was originally orientated to large farmers, its
branhes and stockists carrying some 6,000 different items of merchandise
also provide a valuable service to smallholder agriculture. In 1974, more
than half of the total KFA sales were to small farmers.
5.        Despite these major inroads into smallholder agriculture, in the
medium and low potential areas including parts of Nyanza and Western pro-
vince (outside the Project) the KFA network is relatively thin among
smallholders. Many of the appointed traders either do not function at all
or carry very small quantities of inputs. Several factors reinforce the poor
availability of modern inputs and in particular fertilizer in these areas.
The fixed retail margins do not cover the cost of transportation to areas
poorly accessible by transport; the demand for fertilizer, and hence the
turnover is usually limited. Further, in the less comnercialized areas, the
merchants also have to incur additionaL costs in selling smaller quantities
than the 40, 50 and 100 kg bags in which the fertilizer is packaged. In
the past when inputs were sold on credit provided bv the AFC, there have
also been delays in reimbursement of merchants by the AFC. This situation



ANNEX 4
Page 2
has improved considerably in the recent months. Nevertheless, where the
scope for cash sales is limited and sales are contingent upon AFC credit,
stockists have been reluctant to deal in inputs. To some extent the
Project would help correct this situation through increasing demand for
fertilizer. However, it is necessary that expansion of input distri-
bution through private stockists not be held back through such policies as
regulation of market margins at excessively low levels, since traders would
promote cash sales of inputs in situations where profitability of innova-
tions is demonstrated and credit is not a constraint among smallholders.
6.        The second major channel of input distribution has been the coopera-
tive movement. Hitherto cooperatives and settlements have obtained supplies
of inputs from merchandisers against forward orders of their members, organized
on a society basis and distributed by them largely for cash crops. Following
the shortages of fertilizers experienced in 1973/74, and because of the size of
the operation, the Department of Cooperatives has initiated a merchandising
branch of the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives (KNFC). This commenced
operations in the 1975 long rainy season primarily handling fertilizers. In
principle, this new system can play an important role in ensuring timely supply
of inputs to cooperative members. However, for it to be effective, consider-
able development of management capability is necessary both in the KMFC and
in the cooperative unions and societies. Further coordination of extension
advice with cooperative input distribution is needed to ensure that the right
inputs are ordered in the right mix and are available for distribution at the
right time and place. This is where a major gap exists, and this aspect is
highlighted in the proposed Work Plan (Chapter IV and Schedule).
7.        Once the cooperatives become multi-purpose they are also expected
to become the major channel for the distribution of seed, insecticides and
fertilizers for traditionally subsistence crops such as maize and beans.
There is no reason why cash sales of inputs should not be promoted through
cooperatives as well, so as to mobilize rural savings.
8.        Though it has had obvious weaknesses, the cooperative movement
is slowly being recognized as a suitable organization for input-output
handling. Due to the history of mismanagement, the Cotton Board, the other
possible channel for input distribution, does not enjoy a similar status.
Nor does the Cotton Board have any unique competence, either in terms of man-
power or a marketing network to handle inputs for crops other than cotton.
The negative attitude against the Board seems to be so strong and the develop-
ment eforts of the cooperatives are so far advanced that an increased role
for the Board in input supply does not seem called for. (Also see Annex 5
on cooperatives for additional reasons in support of this argument). The
project therefore envisages that the input marketing for cotton and other
crops be modeled after the Kenyan Coffee industry, which uses the Board mainly
for end-product marketing and the cooperative structure for input and produce
marketing.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
9.        However, no cooperative union or society should be allowed to
participate in project activities unless it is equipped with adequate
credit and marketing facilities. The district project coordinator should
draw on the Cotton Board branch manager, the extension staff, the district
agricultural officer and district cooperative officer for advice in determin-
ing type, quantity and timing of inputs needed. The Cotton Board itself would
thus not extend its activities to input marketing, but would play an advisory
role with regard to planning and ordering of inputs and their proper and
timely application.
10.       Total firm forward orders for fertilizers in 1974/75 were approxi-
mately 185,000 tons by all importers, of which some were subsequently
cancelled, and some could be available for export. In addition, consider-
able stocks of fertilizers existed with KNFC in 1975. Although these sur-
pluses were partly a result of unusually high fertilizer prices and the
consequent slackening of demand, given the small size of the marginal
increase expected in overall consumption as a result of the IADP, there is
not expected to be a shortage in the supplies either of fertilizer or of
seed and chemicals to the target population in the first phase of the
project.
Input Pricing
11.       As of March 1976, Government was the only importer of fertilisers.
By requiring a standard farmgate fertilizer price across the country, Govern-
ment is in effect subsidising transport costs to the more remote areas.
Apart from this, there is no policy to subsidise fertiliser prices. In pricing
inputs in the IADP areas there is a dual objective: (i) to further the effic-
iency in the production, processing and marketing of cash crops including in
particular of cotton, currently the major cash crop in Western and Nyanza
provinces and (ii) to improve the productivity of the overall farming system
through use of cotton and other cash crops as a (credit) security. The
pricing and delivery of inputs need to take this duality into account.
12.       The provision of information on expected price and demand develop-
ment both for inputs and crops will be an important part of input marketing.
Such information will be crucial for making allocative decisions, particularly
since the farmer is usually in a poor position to make such forecasts and in
great need of advice.
13.       Nevertheless, if cash crops are to act as anchor crops there has to
be some minimum production of the cash crop. The question as to the appro-
priate level of cash crop production, as well as the extent to which farmers
should be allowed to secure their credit, by growing sunflower, tobacco or
other suitable security crops, will have to be determined on a case-by-case
basis jointly by the extension staff, the District Agricultural Committee and
Cooperatives before approving each applicant for credit.



ANNFX 4
Page 4
14.       As cotton is a seasonal crop the extension advice will depend on
the possible use of cotton as an anchor crop for the particular season and
on the profitability of growing cotton. Prices will have to be fixed on a
year to year basis for both inputs and outputs and advice given on the basis
of price relationships.
15.       It might be valuable to investigate the possibilities of subsidiz-
ing certain inputs temporarily, as for instance, spraying services, to get
higher acceptance. However, apart from such temporary subsidies, input
prices should be fixed strictly on cost basis and should depend on the effi-
ient operations of the input marketing organization in question. By the
same token, official prices of output should reflect market costing and
should be designed to provide the farmer enough incentive to produce.



ANNEX 5
Page 1
KENYA
INTERGRATED AGRICULTURAT. DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Cooperative Mcvement in Kenya
1.        The cooperative movement in Kenya is expected to play a pivotal
role in the Project. Through its various agencies the movement will be res-
ponsible for distribution of most of the seasonal credit to be provided under
the Project and of related inputs including fertilizer, insecticides and labor,
as the credit will be distributed in kind. The marketing and processing of
a major portion of the output to be produced under the Project is also ex-
pected to be handled by the cooperative movement.
Cooperative Growth
2.        The importance of the cooperative movement has been on the increase
over time but has varied significantly among crops and regions. At the begin-
ning of the 1974-78 Development Plan, there were an estimated 1,060 active
cooperative societies in Kenya, with a total membership of about 450,000 people.
Of these, about 800 societies served rural areas including the settlement
schemes. Approximately 600 were agricultural marketing societies, most con-
centrating on a single commodity, such as coffee, pyrethrum, cotton and dairy
products. About 90 percent of the pyrethrum, 50 percent of the coffee and 25%
of all milk sold by farmers in Kenya is estimated to be marketed through the
primary societies. To support the primary societies, about 40 District Co-
operative UJnions have been established which provide centralized services
including accounting, book-keeping, transport, storage, credit and savings
facilities.
3.        The 1974-78 Development Plan document states the Government's commit-
ment to consolidate and expand the role of the cooperative movement in Kenya
during and beyond the plan period. The Cooperative Development Policy of Kenya
is outlined in detail in the Government's Sessional Paper No. 8 of 1970 which has
been up-dated by Sessional Paper No. 14 of 1975.
The Primary Societies and the Unions
4.        Most of the primary cooperative societies are marketing and process-
ing societies, organized to market farm produice. Although most specialize in
a single commodity, some societies have expanded their operations to cover
marketing of several commodities. Some have also expanded their role into
activities other than marketing, for example, supply of farm inputs including
fertilizer, seed, and pesticide; adninistration of the tractor hire service,
and cattle dipping facilities; and provision of short-term credit. Such
societies are known as "multi-purpose" cooperatives. It is the Government's
intention to convert all the rural cooperatives over the next several vears



ANNEX 5
Page 2
into multi-commodity, multi-purpose societies. Such a trend is already
visible, since in some cases, 2 or more single commodity cooperatives in
the same geographical area have been merged into single societies at the
suggestion of the Department of Cooperative Development.
5.        There is another major category of cooperatives in the rural
areas, known as the "land purchase" cooperatives, formed to facilitate
purchase and operation of large farms that were previously owned by
Europeans. In addition, there are some societies that are concerned with
livestock production, fishing and handicrafts.
6.        Cooperative unions coordinate the activities of primary societies
in a given area, and usually, are related to a single commodity. On behalf
of the primary societies, the unions also deal with the countrywide cooper-
atives, marketing boards and the Cooperative Bank, and provide societies
assistance in keeping accounts, planning, purchase and storage activities.
Like the cooperative societies, the cooperative unions are also expected to
diversify their activities in the future, becoming multi-commodity and multi-
purpose unions. Most primary societies do not have any hired clerical or
management staff. Such staff is usually hired and paid by the unions.
Societies do employ some laborers and factory workers.
7.        A farmer can be a member of several cooperative societies and
receive services and credit facilities from all of them. The need for multi-
ple membership is expected to disappear in the future with the development
of multi-commodity cooperatives.
Countrywide Cooperatives
8.        Apart from the primary societies and unions, there are several
countrywide cooperative organizations. These include the Kenya Planters
Cooperative Union, which undertakes the marketing and processing of coffee,
the Kenya Cooperative Creameries Limited, which carries out the majority of
milk marketing and enjoys a near monopoly in marketing of milk in major
cities, the Horticultural Cooperative Union which markets vegetables and
fruit and the Kenya Farmers Association (Cooperative) Limited, which plays a
major role in supplying farm inputs and in marketing farm products, the
last involving mostly large scale commercial farmers. The Cooperative Bank
of Kenya is a national cooperative that provides banking services to other
cooperative organizations. Finally, there is an apex organization of the
cooperatives, the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives, to which many
of the primary societies, unions and the other countrywide cooperatives be-
long.
The Department of Cooperatives Development
9.        The Department of Cooperative Development was established with the
aim of promoting the development of cooperatives, providing guidance and
exercising stipervision and controL.  Under the Cooperative Societies Act of



ANNEX 5
Page 3
1966 and the Cooperative Act and the Cooperative Rules (1969), the Commissioner
for Cooperative Development has been given wide powers to regulate the co-
operatives, including the right to remove the committee of a society or replace
it for a limited time, to enforce amalgamation of uneconomic societies, to en-
sure that expenditures are kept within approved budgets, and to prescribe
policies governing the credit activities of the cooperative unions and societies.
10.       The Department promotes development through assistance in management,
implementation of accounting systems, credit schemes and training programs for
the cooperative staff. A major function of the Department is to audit the
accounts of the various cooperative unions (and, in some cases, societies). Tn
addition to the Commissioner of Cooperative Development, who is the Registrar
of Cooperative Societies and has overall responsibility for the cooperative
movement in Kenya, there are Assistant Commissioners for cooperative develop-
ment (or the Provincial Cooperative Officers) who supervise the cooperative
movement in the provinces, and the District Cooperatives Officers who coordinate
and control the cooperatives in the districts.
11.       The Department of Cooperative Development had issued a directive in
1974 to all primary societies and unions, setting an upper limit on the total
overhead costs and commissions charged to the farmers by unions and societies.
The unions and societies together are allowed to charge no more than 20% of
the value of the farmer's produce handled by the cooperative organizations,
(this includes commissions for marketing, input supply services, transportation
costs and office overheads, but does not include interest on the credit supplied).
However, there are a number of societies which are currently exceeding the set
limit on overhead expenses; the Department has not taken action against then
so far on the assumption that the situation can be improved gradually through
the Nordic program which is directed to correcting the overall deficiencies in
the cooperative movement including inexperienced and poorly trained management,
inadequate book-keeping systems, and generally low level of public account-
ability.
Technical Assistance
12.       The "Nordic program" is a sizeable program of technical assistance
from a consortium of Scandinavian countries. It has been operating in Kenya
since 1967 with the objective of developing and consolidating the cooperative
movement. About 40 advisers are currently assisting various cooerative organ-
izations throughout Kenva under this program.  Mkost of them are assigned to one
of the cooperative unions; about 5 are at the headquarters of the Department of
Cooperatives and 4 are with the Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives, work-
ing on developing adequate accounting systems and book-keeping, organization of
credit and planning of the cooperative activities. The Nordic technical assist-
ance program has been substantially effective in strengthening several of the
societies, particularly in the coffee and pyrethrum areas. This program is now
heing slowly phased out and, according to the present plan, is expected to
terminate after 1977. The IJSAID loan (which is taking the role of the forerun-
ner of the Integrated AgriculturaL Development Project on a small scale), has a



ANNEX 5
Page 4
a modest technical assistance component providing for approximately 10
peace corps volunteers and other advisers who will work in areas adjacent
to the Project area.
The Kenya National Federation of Cooperatives
13.       This apex organization of all the cooperatives in Kenya came into
existence in 1964. Although the charter of KNFC envisaged a much wider
role, in recent years its activities have been restricted to the formal
representation of the cooperatives in their relations with the Government,
information and publicity including a weekly radio program and publication of
a quarterly news magazine "Mshiriki wa Kenya", training for the junior staff
of the cooperative societies, group insurance to cover the property owned by
its member cooperatives and supply of stationery and supply of farm inputs to
the cooperative societies had been added to its activities. The KNFC is able
to purchase inputs at bulk rates and hopes to distribute them to the various
societies at competitive prices. At present, only about 10 percent of the
total farm inputs sold to Kenya farmers is being supplied through the KNFC
channel. Participation of KNFC in merchandising of inputs is also hoped
to yield the additional benefit of stabilizing input prices and guaranteeing
timely supplies. To be effective in this regard, the KNFC will have to
strengthen its management significantly and assistance would be provided
through the Project in the form of equipment, vehicles and staffing, to enable
KNFC to fulfill its Project role.



ANNEX 6
Page 1
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAI, DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Credit and Credit Institutions
Type of Coverage
1.        All smallholders selected for participation in the Project would
be eligible for seasonal credit. The Project would also have a subsistence
farmers credit (SFCF) through which an effort would be made to introduce a
cash crop to subsistence farmers.  The objective would be to incorporate such
farmers in the main stream of institutional services. The subsistence farmer
fund is described later in this Annex. From year 5 on the fund is expected to
benefit about 3,500 farmers per year. The total number of smallholders ex-
pected to be affected by the Project is estimated at 70,000 (equivalent to
around 324,000 people) cultivating 56,000 holdings.
2.        Credit would be seasonal and would cover both food crops (maize,
beans, potatoes), and cash crops (cotton, passion fruit, groundnuts and
sunflower). In the Project's livestock component, credit for such items as
pasture improvement, construction of water tanks and acquisition of oxen or
improved zebu bulls would continue to be made available through existing
credit schemes and will not be covered by this Project.
3.        Credit would primarily be made available to farmers associated
with cooperative societies that have monopoly buying or processing positions,
although participating cooperatives would not necessarily be limited to the
specific crops involved in the Project. Farmers who live in areas not served
by cooperatives, or had deposited their title deeds with AFC to secure previous
loans, or are borrowing from AFC for non-seasonal purposes would be able to
receive credit through AFC. The relative amounts of cooperative and AFC
lending would be agreed annually through the mechanism of Work Plans (Schedule).
Credit Channels
4.        Money for seasonal credit would be loaned by Government to the
Cereals and Sugar Finance Corporation (CSFC)1/ for 20 years to operate a
revolving fund for which funds would be on-lent to the Cooperative Bank and
AFC for 2 year periods to enable them to extend credit to farmers (see paras.
48 to 56).
Rural Cooperatives
5.        In December 1975, societies representing crop marketing, livestock
including dairy, multipurpose and farm purchase numbered approximately 1,500
with total membership exceeding 600,000. However, the credit operations of
the cooperative movement are relatively young, and to date, there are 13
1/   CSFC is a small corporation established under the Cereals and Sugar
Finance Corporation Act (L.K. Cap. 329), located within the Treasury, and
used by the Kenya Government ailmost excltusively as a channel for on-lending
Project funds to other institutions.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
unions with 126 member societies which are providing, in addition to their
primary marketing or production function, the services of supplying inputs,
credit through the Cooperative Production Credit Scheme (CPCS) and a sav-
ings plan. A further 600 societies are active in providing limited crop
advances together with minimal supplies of inputs.
6.        The CPCS scheme has been implemented mainly in Central and Eastern
Provinces and, to a lesser extent in Western and Nyanza Provinces. Approxi-
mately 60,000 loans were made under CPCS during 1970-73, totalling Ksh 31 mil-
lion, an average of about 520 shillings per borrower. The societies operating
the scheme at the end of 1973 involved an active membership of 205,000 farmers.
The repayment record has exceeded 90 percent.
7.        The Department of Cooperative Development proposes to provide the
CPCS and savings schemes to between 290,000 and 335,000 members, i.e. 48-56
percent of the total membership by 1978. It is the aim of the Project to assist
in ensuring that this target is reached, particularly by strengthening coverage
in Western and Nyanza province, while achieving the good record of the CPCS
scheme realized elsewhere.
8.        Approximately 30 percent of the cooperative unions and societies now
operating the CPCS and savings program have acquired capital approaching double
the amount required by these unions for on-lending to members for short-term
lending purposes. Finance for short-term credit is not currently a constraint
for these, (generally) the oldest and most efficient unions, although it is
still a constraint for others.
Agricultural Credit through the Cooperative System
The Cooperative Bank
9.        Established in 1968, the Cooperative Bank of Kenya is a specialized
financial institution serving the cooperative system, and offers cooperative
societies banking services designed to meet their special needs. The Coop-
erative Bank has one office, located in Nairobi. It is registered both as
a commercial bank and as a cooperative organization. It does not provide a
full range of banking services and has no individual depositors, but serves as
the financial arm of the cooperative system. It offers its customers (limited
to cooperative societies) banking services designed to meet their specific
needs, and is also designed to support Government efforts to strengthen the
cooperative movement.  The aim of the Cooperative Bank is to pool funds
generated by cooperatives and to make them available to the cooperative
movement. The Bank was founded because it was felt that cooperative societies
in the commercial banking system were large in relation to the services
extended to cooperatives by commercial banks and that the volume of coopera-
tive transactions was sufficient to justify the existence of a specialized
bank. For day-to-day convenience, cooperative societies generally maintain
accounts at a commercial bank branch in the nearest commercial center. The
Cooperative Bank lends to societies under the Cooperative Production Credit
Scheme for crop finance, and also provides a limited amount of medium-term
finance for coffee factories, cotton ginneries and other investments under-
taken by cooperatives including land purchase, buildings and vehicles.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
10.        Under the Cooperative Production Credit Scheme, individual farmers
and primarY societies may borrow in kind from their unions under a standard
formula, subject to the approval of the society's credit committee (which in-
cludes the society's management and the local extension officer). The farmer's
borrowing limit is set at 2/3 of his annual production of cash crop handled by
the society, based on experience over the past three years; the total credit
extended is limited by the amount of credit available to the society from its
own members' savings and other cooperative societies. Societies lend against
estimated crop proceeds and require that letters of undertaking covering re-
payments be obtained from statutory buying boards before credit is extended.
Each borrower must also provide security in the form of two guarantors.
11.       In addition to the CPCS loans, some cooperative societies also ad-
vance seasonal loans amounting to up to 10 percent of the value of the farmer's
estimated cash crop production. This credit can be available to the farmer up
to 4 times a year, but under the condition that his previous borrowing under
this 10 percent scheme has been fully repaid before he can borrow the next
time. This scheme is supported by the Cooperative Bank through the "crop
advances" (short-term credit of 3-6 months maturity) from the Bank to the
unions and societies.
12.       The individual unions and societies are not allowed by the Department
of Cooperatives to relax the conditions under which credit is granted to the
farmers. The only exceptions to this rule are the experimental credit schemes
tried as part of special development assistance, principally in the Special
Rural Development Project (SRDP) areas.
Lending Rates through the Cooperatives
Cooperative Bank to Unions/Societies
13.       In recent years the Cooperative Bank has been borrowing at the rate
of 4-1/2 percent from the Government. Lending rates from Cooperative Bank to
Unions or Societies are:
(1) CPCS loans - prime rate (up to 18 months) approximately
8 percent in July 1975.
(2) 3-6 month crop advances - about 9 percent.
(3)  Medium term loans for implements, vehicles, etc. (2 years) -
about 9 percent.
(4) Long term loans (2-5 years) for land purchases, processing
plants - 9 to 10 percent under reducing balances.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
14.       Longest maturity of loans from the Cooperative Bank is 5 years.  The
bad debts of the Cooperative Bank have so far been very few, with a repayment
rate of about 98 percent. This has of course been largely a result of limiting
the cooperative activities to the traditional export crops, most of which re-
quire further processing and therefore, facilitate centralized marketing.
Unions and Societies to Farmers
15.       On short-term loans (including CPCS), the unions/societies charge
farmers a margin of 2% to 4% for their services. No margin is charged on
medium and long-term loans.
Participants
16.       Eighteen of the cooperative unions in Kenya participate in the CPCS
scheme; about 225 primary societies administer the CPCS to their members,
largely in coffee and pyrethrum growing areas.
17.       As of June 30, 1974, K.Sh. 123 million of the total Cooperative Bank
funding consisted of members' deposits. K.Sh. 7 million consisted of fully
paid shares of 100 Sh each, K.Sh. 3 million was made up of long-term loans
from Kenya Government, and K.Sh. 0.5 million was from reserves. The bulk of
these funds (K.Sh. 97 million or 72%) was held as money at call and short
notice, K.Sh. 33 million (24%) in loans and overdrafts, and the rest in other
asset forms. A statement of sources and allocation of funds is in Table 1
and a detailed Income and Expenditure Account is in Table 2.
The Agricultural Finance Corporation
18.       The other major credit institution which would be involved in the
Project is the Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC). It was established in
1963 as a statutory body to take over the credit functions of the two Boards
of Agriculture then serving the Scheduled (European) and Nonscheduled (African)
Areas, and to work in close cooperation with the Land and Agricultural Bank
established in 1931. Under the AFC Act of 1969, AFC was reconstituted with
wider powers and took over the operations and assets and liabilities of the
Land and Agricultural Bank. The present functions of the corporation are to
assist in the development of agriculture and agricultural industries by making
loans to farmers, cooperative societies, incorporated group representatives,
private companies public bodies, local authorities and other persons engaging
in agriculture or agricultural industries. At present, AFC is the primary
agricultural credit institution in Kenya and it is the only lender serving the
farm sector through a variety of lending programs to the commercially oriented
farms large or small.
19.       AFC currently extends credit under three large-scale schemes sup-
ported by external donors. The first scheme covers land purchase and develop-
ment loans financed under the British Land Transfer Program. The major compo-
nents of the transfer program were: (i) settlement schemes, involving pre-
dominantly smallholdings but also including some medium to large-scaLe farms



ANNEX 6
Page 5
and some cooperative units; (ii) the British Land Transfer Assisted Owners
Scheme thrni.gh which transFer of farms; ' med by UK citizens and located out-
side settlement areas was facilitated by providing funds to the Kenya Govern-
ment for on-lending to AFC; and (iii) the Stamp Purchase Plan which was ini-
tiated in 1966 to finance the purchase and transfer of tihe remaining British
owned farms which comprised some of the best farms in the country and to en-
sure their contribution as single units in order to safeguard what was con-
sidered the unique economic advantage of this scale of activity.
20.       The second of the large-scale schemes involves ranching loans funded
jointly by IDA, Sweden, and USAID. These are designed to assist in the devel-
opment of different types of ranching organizations (group, company, indivi-
dual and commercial ranching enterprises) and involves the establishment and
improvement of technical services for the ranching sector plus investment in
livestock routes and marketing facilities.
21.       The third scheme is comprised of the large farm development programs
supported by the overseas development finance institution of the Federal
Republic of Germany, Kreditanstalt fur Wiederaufbau (KFW), to provide funds
for the establishment of state-owned animal breeding farms and for the ration-
alization of large-scale African farms in the Trans Nzoia District. Funds
were made available to AFC for on-lending to a number of large-scale African
farmers in the Kitale area wnose farms it was expected could benefit from in-
jections of capital. An agricultural team provided under German foreign
assistance is operating in this area, and loan funds were provided to support
their extension activities.
22.       AFC is also extending credit under two small-scale programs which
include smallholder development projects funded by IDA and KFW. These projects
were designed to provide credit for on-farm development and to assist in di-
versifying and improving crops and animal husbandry practices of selected
smallholders. Included also is a small poultry development element, financing
for farm machinery and equipment, and a technical assistance element providing
for an agricultural credit survey in Kenya.
23.       In addition to those schemes supported by external donors, AFC
operates small-scale and large-scale lending schemes from its own resources
(mainly from capital provided by the Government). From its own resources, AFC
also operates a large-scale lending program which provides both land purchase
and development loans as a supplement to those provided by the British Land
Transfer Program. Although no firm breakdown of large-scale loans by purpose
is available, it is estimated that aoproximately 60 percent of all large-scale
loans irrespective of the source of financing are for land purchase, about 30
percent are for on-farm development and about 7 percent are for repayment of
outstanding debts other than those owed to AFC. These debt consolidating
loans are granted to enable AFC to obtain a charge over the farmer's assets,
and such a consolidation is generally considered to be to the farmer's advan-
tage as the AFC loan is normally at a lower rate of interest than is obtainable
elsewhere and it is usually accompanied by farm planning services.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
24.       Small-scale programs generally provide funds in amounts up to KShs
10,000, while large-scale schemes are typically much larger and often finance
quite different farm enterprises. The ratio of large-scale to small-scale
loans outstanding as of March 31, 1973 stood at 6:1, although of course, AFC has
many more small-scale than large-scale borrowers. AFC's large-scale portfolio
includes land purchase loans which are extended for a 30-year term, while prac-
tically all of the small-scale loans are for land and enterprise development,
on terms ranging from 3 to 10 years; the small-scale portfolio thus has a more
rapid turnover than the large-scale portfolio. Due to AFC's requirement of
land-title as the security for loans (described below) few low income, small
farmers are willing or able to surrender land titles for a small amount of
seasonal credit. Moreover AFC makes its loans to individual farmers and not
on a group (cooperative) basis and this severely limits the number of farmers
which it can reach. Therefore even after the recent decentralization of AFC's
credit administration, which has simplified the lending procedures, the
pattern of lending has not changed to include larger numbers of low income
small farmers.
25.       AFC lends on a secured basis, generally against mortgages on land,
and in certain instances charges are also taken against movable property.
In general, the coverage should not be less than 1-1/2 times the approved
loan. When the loan is in default, the entire amount outstanding becomes due
automatically under a clause included in AFC's loan agreements. However, at
stages short of foreclosure AFC does not exercise its right to call the entire
amount outstanding. The Corporation has recently instituted a policy of
paying interest (at approximately 5% p.a.) to borrowers who make prepayments
through monthly payments to AFC in anticipation of larger sums which fall due
quarterly.
26.       Management and Organization.  AFC was transferred to the office of
the President in June 1970, but no changes were made in the composition of its
Board of Directors. The M4inistry of Finance and Planning and the Ministry of
Agriculture are represented on the Board by their Permanent Secretaries, and
the Secretary of Finance and Planning currently serves as Chairman of the Board.
In addition to the two Permanent Secretaries, the board consists of between
four and six Ministerial appointees (two of whom are required to have expe-
rience in banking or finance) drawn from official and private sources.
27.       AFC has a fairly centralized structure, with a Head Office located
in Nairobi and a network of 32 branch offices which provide coverage of
Kenya's principal agricultural districts.   During the past two years some
decentralization has taken place in an effort to spread the administrative
burden and to use the existing staff more effectively. Four Area Managers
play a vital role in the decentralization process, as each is responsible
for the guidance and supervision of offices in his geographical area of re-
sponsibility. Three of the area managers were provided by USAID and the



ANNEX 6
Page 7
fourth was provided by German Aid. Most of the loan approvals for small-scale
loans are made at the Head Office on the recommendations of branch managers,
although an initial screening takes place in the field in two stages. In the
first stage extension staff advise farmers to obtain an AFC loan application
and assist them to fill it in; at the second stage District Loan Committees
(whliich include branch managers and extension officers) advise AFC's head
office on the credit worthiness of prospective borrowers in their respec-
tive districts.
28.       Financial Resources.  The bulk of AFC's financial resources is pro-
vided by the Government. It consists partly of irredeemable or ownership
capital, some of which is interest-bearing (originally belonging to the pre-
decessor corporation, the Land and Agricultural Bank), and partly of interest-
free grants, and redeemable or long-term debt capital. As of March 31, 1974,
irredeemable capital amounted to Ksh 127 million (US$16 million), of which
Ksh 47 million (US$5.8 million) was interest free grants. Redeemable loans
which include proceeds of loans to Government from IDA and other external
sources which were on-lent to AFC amounted to Ksh 151 million (US$18.8 mil-
lion) as of that date. Prior to the effectiveness of the IDA Smallholder
Credit Project in 1967, AFC was charged interest by Government at 6% p.a.,
but since that date all funds have been provided at 3.5% p.a. in accordance
with the terms of the IDA credit which stipulated that Government would not
charge more than 3.5% p.a. on any future loans to AFC made while AFC is in-
debted with respect to that project. For fiscal year 1973 the blend of
interest payable averaged 4.5% on interest bearing capital or 3.5% on total
resources including interest free grants. Financial statements for 1971 to
1974 are in Table 3. AFC actually made a profit of Ksh 4 million in the
first half of 1975/76 and now expects to make a profit for the whole of that
year (Table 4). In any case, the impact of this Project on AFC's finances
is expected to be small. Project credit funds expected to pass through AFC
amount to only 10% of AFC's preProject financial resources. AFC is profit-
able now, and Government seems determined to keep it so.
29.       The allocation of credit for inputs to Cooperatives or AFC would be
proposed in Work Plans and agreed between Government and the Bank. Present
indications are that K.Sh 28 million or 25% of credit for inputs in this
Project would pass through AFC, and the rest through the Cooperatives.
This represents about 10% increase of AFC's net loans to farmers in 1975.
To cope with this increase in activity, AFC's staff is being strengthened
under the Project with a Head Office accountant, 3 senior administrative
officers, 20 field officers and 16 clerical officers.
30.       Through its 33 branches, AFC is reaching some 16,500 small-scale
farmers. 1/  Historically the mair constraints to increasing the number of
small scale loans have been the time consuming aspect of dealing with indi-
vidual farmers, resistance of AFC to small and administratively expensive
loans, and, particularly, the lengthy administrative procedures, involving
depositing of title deeds, which has resulted in an average time lapse of
? months between application and disbursements.
/ Defined as loans not exceeding KShs 10,000 per indivdual.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
31.       This last constraint is now being removed by way of decentralizing
lending authority to the branch level, and restructuring the loan applica-
tion procedure. In addition, the AFC as a matter of policy has agreed to
participate in providing seasonal loans to small-scale farmers until such
time as the cooperative society organization can satisfy this need. As a
result, loan processing should now take 3-4 weeks for short-term loans, and
2-3 weeks for small-scale seasonal credit. The decentralization program has
been introduced in 7 AFC branches in the Mount Kenya area (November 1974).
The Western area was to follow in February 1975, with the other 3 areas being
gradually phased in thereafter, in line with program objectives.
32.       AFC's loans to smallholders are secured by title deeds; the average
size of these loans has previously ranged from Kshs 3,000 to 4,000 for both
medium term loans (2 years) 1/ and crop financing. AFC has only provided
seasonal credit as an agent of Central Agricultural Board, in the form of
Guaranteed Minimum Return (GMR) loans, for wheat and maize only; these bene-
fit only larger farmers (i.e. basic requirement minimum of 15 areas of wheat
or maize.
Capacity of Credit Agencies
33.       The two existing channels for short-term loans and seasonal credit
to small-scale farmers, the Cooperatives and AFC, provide sufficient geo-
graphical coverage for proposed Project lending. The immediate additional
capacity of AFC, which involves processing of applications and limited super-
vision in conjunction with the Ministry of Agriculture extension staff, has
been estimated at 300 applicatons per AFC branch officer. Cooperative
Societies are expected to handle a similar number. At present the small-
holder credit situation in Kenya is somewhat confused, because of the over-
lapping roles played by AFC and the cooperative societies. AFC operates
efficiently and provides a valuable lending source in areas inadequately
covered by cooperative societies. On a district by district basis, the two
sources adequately cover the proposed Project locations. However, in the
long term, their respective roles in smallholder lending would have to be more
clearly defined.
34.       The field extension service of the Ministry of Agriculture should
be able to determine the effectiveness of the credit system since it coor-
dinates the bodies involved and leads the loan approval committees.   The
extension staff should provide continuing contact with the farmers to advise
on agricultural production. So far, the extension service has been unable
to determine credit effectiveness and coordinate credit administration.
The Project hopes to rectify this deficiency by improved organization,
training and supervision.
1/   IDA Credit 105 experience; most of these loans were for:  2 dairy
cows, fencing, a water tank, dairy equipment and some crop develop-
ment (from:  Credit 105-KE, Completion  Report of July 11, 1974).

Page 9
Financial Requirements
35.       The farm budgets have estimated the following requirements of
seasonal inputs for Project crop development:
Crop                   Ksh/ha                Crop                  Ksh/ha
Maize                             911
Groundnut                       1,120               Cotton                   653

Passion Fruit                   3,340
The weighted average credit requirement is some KSh 762 per ha for crop
production.
36.       Total seasonal and short term credit requirements during the Project
are calculated as KSh112 million 1/ for the 5-year Project period. 2/  These
funds would be channelled largely through the Cooperative System, but also
through AFC.
Training and Technical Assistance
AFC
37.       The decentralization program of AFC, which will improve speed and
capacity of handling seasonal credit, is now being implemented. Additional
staff, mostly trained in agriculture, are being hired; under the Project,
provision would be made for additional accountants, supervisors and book-
keeping staff, in addition to vehicles for supervision and training programs
(para 28).
1/   This consists of 75% of the total requirement for physical inputs
and hired labor (Annex 10, Tables 11, 12 and 13).
2/   There are only 4 farming years in the 5-year Project period.



ANNEX 6
Page 10
Cooperatives
38.       The Cooperative sector is at present being assisted with about 40
expatriate (Nordic) staff: most of these are assigned to credit (8), account-
ing (10) and planning (11). This team is due to be reduced by approximately
ten members by 1977. Training for the cooperative societies -- managers, book-
keepers -- is being carried out at the Kenya Cooperative College, Langata.
The capacity of this College would be adequate for the needs of the Coopera-
tive system. Unfortunately, however, over 50 percent of its graduates seek
employment elsewhere. This situation arises from the relatively low salary
structure of the cooperatives, and the absence of an identifiable career
structure within the system that would enable managers to be posted to
different societies and gain additional experience. This matter is now
receiving Government attention, and is not expected to adversely affect the
manpower requirements of the Project (see Annex 2).
39.       The cooperatives are most vulnerable on the bookkeeping and account-
ing aspects. If, upon inspection, they are judged to be deficient in the
following areas;
(1)  development of accounting systems, with possibilities for
management control;
(2)  training of bookkeepers to adequately maintain accounts
as developed under 1; and
(3)  training of controllers to check performance of bookkeepers.
It is recommended that the technical assistance function being carried out by
Nordics should be extended beyond 1977 either through them or through an
alternate external assistance program. This aspect would be covered in the
proposed Work Plans.
Subsistence Farmers Credit Fund (SFCF)
40.       Most project farmers are unlikely to have ready access to AFC,
because of the small size of their individual seasonal credit requirements.
The farmer who already has a marketable surplus would be assisted for his
credit needs by the cooperative system (or AFC). However, the real subsis-
tence farmer, i.e. the farmer without a marketable surplus, would not be
serviced either by the Cooperatives 1/ or by AFC; and there is at present no
institution designed specially to deal with the credit needs of this class of
farmers.
1/   For a cooperative society member to receive credit, he must have
marketed his output through that society for 2-3 years. This marketed
surplus, together with the associated proven farming ability, are used
as a basis for deciding the size of the loan. By definition, therefore,
subsistence farmers cannot get credit from the cooperatives under existing
rules.



ANNEX 6
Page 11
41.       The Project, therefore, proposes to expand the ongoing pilot scheme
which would enable subsistence farmers to produce a marketable surplus, which
they would sell through the cooperatives. They would then qualify for seasonal
credit from cooperative societies in the normal way. After a few years of
on-farm development using cooperative credit, the farmer may also become
eligible for AFC loans (both short and medium term).
42.       In order to enable the subsistence farmer to produce a surplus,
and hence something to market cooperative societies, through the approved
suppliers and extension service, would make credit available under a new small
farmer scheme.
43.       Thereafter if a farmer (i) has successfully followed the advice
of the extension service, and (ii) has marketed his surplus through the
Co-operative Society, would be eligible for credit from the Co-operatives
to finance his inputs. Farmers who have not been able to benefit from this
opportunity would not be further assisted.
Organization
44.       For the assistance program for subsistence farmers to be effective
and for it to reach the people for whom it is designed, the following steps
are proposed:
(i) classification of farmers into subsistence and commercial
categories -- this would be defined by central Ministry of
Agriculture in conjunction with provincial officers, who would
also suggest through Work Plans, a phasing programme by locations.
A legal definition of the subsistence farmer for this purpose is
contained in paragraph 56(a) below.
(ii)  screening of applicants -- this would be undertaken at district
level in accordance with the district agricultural committees;
(iii)  entering memberships for acceptors at local Co-operative
Society;
(iv)  supplying input packages consisting of seed, pesticides and
fertiliser;
(v) regular follow-up by extension service; and
(vi)  mandatory marketing through Co-operative Societies.
45.       Classification of farmers, and screening would be undertaken by
the members of district agricultural committees, including:
District Commissioner,
District Agricultural Officer,
District Land and Farm Management Officer,
Co-operative Union Members (2).



ANNEX 6
Page 12
The committee's decision would be based on advice from the following
who would not have voting power:
-    Chief of Division
-    Sub-Chief of Location
-    Division Assistant Agricultural Officer
-    Extension Staff (2)
46.       Like the rest of the Scheme for farm inputs SFCF would commence at
the start of the second Project year (i.e. March/April 1977) and reach approxi-
mately 3,500 farmers cultivating 2,800 holdings out of the Project total of
70,000 farmers and 56,000 holdings as follows:-

(5% of total Project objective) the program will provide a valuable basis
from which to expand this approach during subsequent phases. The cost per
farmer would be exactly the same as for other Project farmers (see Annex 10,
Tables 11, 12 abd 13).
48.        The proceeds of the Credit, the Loan and the BADEA Loan lent by
the Government to CSFC shall be repaid within twenty years including five
years' grace.

of the Credit, the Loan and the BADEA Loan and the maximum repayment periods
in respect thereof shall be as follows:
