50.       Loans to farmers shall be repayable in the shortest period consis-
tent with the farmer's ability to repay in view of the crop for which credit
is extended, and amortization payments shall be calculated so as to coincide
as far as practicable with the times at which the farmer's crops, if properly
cultivated, will be ready for sale.
51.       The CSFC shall lend annually to AFC and the Cooperative Bank, as
required by them for their respective lending programs under the project:
(a)  amounts of principal and interest repaid to the CSFC under the
Subsidiary Loan Agreement and;
(b)  such additional amounts as are equivalent to the proceeds of the
Credit, the Loan and the BADEA Loan withdrawn by the Government
for purposes of providing credit to farmers in accordance with
Work Plans.
CSFC's obligation in respect of the foregoing paragraph 51(a)
shall expire not earlier than three years after the last withdrawal referred
to in paragraph 51(b).



ANNEX 6
Page 14
52.       Funds relent to farmers shall be utilized to provide farmers,
on average, 75% of the variable costs of planting, cultivating, harvesting and
transporting the crops in respect of which credit is sought. Not more than 25%
of the amount to be loaned will be provided in cash.
53.       The standard loan agreements utilized by AFC and cooperative unions
and societies for lending to farmers shall be acceptable to the Association.
54.       CSFC, AFC and the Cooperative Bank shall each be committed to take
such action as is required in order to enable and assist the Borrower in
meeting its obligations under the Development Credit Agreement, including, in
the case of the Cooperative Bank, ensuring adherence to the criteria set out
in paragraph 56 below in its lending to cooperative societies and unions.
55.       In respect of credit for inputs extended in kind, farmers' loan
accounts shall not be charged in amounts exceeding the market price for such
inputs in the area where the farm involved is located.
56.       The criteria for lending to farmers shall be required to include,
except as the Association shall otherwise agree:
(a) In respect of the Subsistence Farmers Credit Fund:
(i) Farmer's agricultural land holdings shall not exceed
8.5 hectares;
(ii) Farmer's net farm income for past year shall not have
exceeded K.Sh.l,000 and farmer's net income for past
year shall not have exceeded K.Sh.5,000; and
(iii) Farmer shall not have previously received substantial
agricultural credit, except under the Project.
(b) In respect of all other credit provided to farmers under the
Project (conventional credit);
(i) Farmer's agricultural land holdings shall not exceed
8.5 hectares; and
(ii) Farmer's net income for past year shall not have exceeded
K.Sh.15,000.
(c) In respect of all credit provided to farmers under the Project
for cotton, no loan shall be made unless the farmer undertakes
to cultivate at least 0.2 hectare of cotton.

ANNEX 7
Pagye 1
KENYA
NTrCRATFTD AGRICTULTURAT D VFT_,OPMFNT PROGRAM  - PHASE T
The Cotton Industry and the Cotton Lint and Seed
Marketing Board _(CLSMB)
Background
1.        Despite its small size in relation to the total Kenyan economy,
the cotton industry is important because in the lowlands (including most of
Coast and Nyanza Provinces, and parts of Central, Eastern and Western Pro-
vinces) cotton is the longest established traditional cash crop. Under this
Project, cotton has the added importance that, because it is inedible and is
marketed through a State monopoly (i.e., the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing
Board) it is the obvious "anchor" crop to use as security for loans to be made
to farmers.
2.        Cotton was introduced to Kenya in 1906 by a private company assisted
by the British Cotton Growers Association, but its commercial development
was so unsuccessful as compared with cotton development in neighboring Uganda
that two of the first three ginneries (in Asembo Bay, and Mtalakisi) were
dismantled and moved to Uganda. Later, with Government and private ginning
company encouragement, cotton growing was adopted by smallholders, and lint
output increased from about 17,000 bales in 1963/64 to about 30,000 bales in
1972/73. Ginnery ownership and management, however, remained in private
(largely expatriate) hands. In recent years it has become established Govern-
ment policy that, in order that farmers reap the maximum return from the
cotton industry, ginneries should be bought out by farmers' cooperatives
except for a few in special areas (e.g., irrigation schemes) which would
remain under private or statutory board ownership. In September 1975, there
were 11 ginneries in Kenya, of which 3 were owned by farmers' cooperatives, 5
by private individuals and companies, and 3 by the Cotton Lint and Seed
Marketing Board (CLSMB). Measures are being taken to transfer ownership of
all ginneries to the cooperatives as soon as possible.
3.        CLSMB is a statutory corporation established in 1955 under the
Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Act. 1/ It has wide-ranging powers for regu-
lating and servicing the cotton industry in Kenya, including for example:
power to buy and sell raw cotton, lint, and seed; prescription of cotton seed
and ginnery standards; licensing and inspection of cotton stores, markets,
ginneries and factories; treatment of cotton diseases; financing ownership
and operation of ginneries; financial technical and other assistance to cotton
growers, growers' cooperatives or ginners; and fixing of prices (with minis-
terial approval) for seed cotton, cotton lint, and cotton seed. As of
September 1975, CLSMB, in addition to providing cotton seed and insecticide on
credit to farmers through their cooperatives, owned three ginneries.
1/   The Act became effective in Nvanza and Co:ist Provinces on July 1, 1955,
and subsequently in other parts of Kenva.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
4.        Deficiencies in CLSMB operations include among others:  absence
of a strong, independent and we1l informed executive capacity; lack of continuity
in manaaement; inadequate agricultural, financial, rosting, credit, pricing
and marketing policies, and over construction ot ginneries left them operating
well below capacity.  These complaints led to the appointment of several com-
mittees of inquiry, including the Cotton Working Party of 1971, and the 11-man
Reorganization Committee which reported in June 1974. These inquiries have,
between them, recommended improvements regarding every aspect of the Boards'
activities. Although most of the recommendations were accepted by the Govern-
ment, many of them were never implemented. Thus in August 1975 Government
appointed yet another Committee of Inquiry to investigate allegations of mis-
management in the Board. As of December 1975, the General Manager and his
four most senior staff were under suspension pending the recommendations of the
Committee of Inquiry. Although Government has not released the recommendations
of this Committee, it is said to have commenced their implimentation.
5.        From discussion with members of the CLSMB, Ministry of Agriculture
staff and members of the Committee of Inquiry, there are a number of areas
of concern which ought to be addressed in a review of the Board's functions
and activities. These points are discussed below. They were discussed
during negotiations, and the most important of them were included in the
Development Credit Agreement.
Composition and the Board
6.        The composition of the Board is prescribed in Section 3(a) of the
Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Act. Representatives of the growers are
appointed by the Minister of Agriculture after consultation with the Central
Agricultural Board. Consideration might be given to the proposition that
the representatives be elected from among the growers in the main cotton
growing areas. Section 3(2)(a) of the Act specifies that representatives
should be designated also from the cotton industry. Consideration should
also be given to the appointment of persons representing different segments
of the industry, thus helping the Board to acquire a knowledge of the com-
petitive market interest of various groups. For example, those appointed
could be chosen both from among local spinners and from exporters.
Functions of the Board
7.        In view of the interest in rationalizing the functions of CLSMB
and in keeping overhead costs within reasonable limits, it might be desirable
to restrict the functions of the Board to (a) local and export sale of lint N
and seed; (b) ginnery inspection and training of gin fitters; (c) purchase of
seed cotton from the cooperative unions and of baled cotton from the ginneries,
and (d) the management and distribution of seed for planting.  These functions
would paralLel to a large extent the similar functions of the Kenva Coffee
Board.  If the functions of the Board were integrated along the lines in-
dicated above, it might then be possible t) reduce the number of headquarters
staFf (and t_) possiblv increase the number c F FielA staff)



ANNEX 7
Page 3
'3.       Mlarketing of Lint and Seed.  Consideration might be given to giving
the Chief Executive of the Board primary responsibility for the marketing of
lint and seed. Ns this is one of the most important functions of the Board,
responsibility should he charged at the highest staff level. The direct
responsibility for overseeing the day-to-day marketing operations could then
be vested in the Deputy Chief. At least one of these officers should have
practical experience of commercial marketing of primary commodities. In order
to help the officers make judgements as to the quantities of lint to be sold,
the time of sale and the price reserves to be set, the Board should subscribe
to a daily advice service which would provide up-to-date information on the
prices of equivalent types and qualities in the main cotton export markets.
9.        The Board might in the future wish to consider selling lint openly
to competing spinners and exporters, with the former given permission to
resell if they wish. Such a procedure would help to create a secondary
market within Kenya and would encourage brokers to reenter the trade. This
secondary market would be a valuable source of price information to the
Board which could assist it in making its own price determinations.
10.       The Chief Executive would benefit greatly by the recruitment of a
qualified Lint Classifier whose main function would be to establish and
maintain the FA (first grade) standlard box for each zone against which AR
(first quality) lint sales are made, as well as the UG (undergrade) box used
to determine the extent to which any sample which is the subject of a quality
claim falls below the FA standard. This Lint Classifier would be the Board's
main spokesman in discussions with claimants with regard to quality defects
(prior to resort to arbitration). As and when the crop expands - particularly
if there is any major development in irrigated cotton - the Classifier could
studv the possibility of establishing premium grades for the various zones,
which could then be the subject of separate sales against a premium standard.
II.       Ginnery Inspection and Training of Gin Fitters.  The Board should
give serious thought to employing two qualified 1/ mechanics trained in gin
fitting and maintenance.  These mechanics would be responsible for the in-
spection of ginnery operations and the correction of faults; they would also
provide on-the-job training for local fitters and mechanics employed by the
ginneries. These staff should then establish a formal inspection and train-
ing routine. Prior to the opening of the ginning season in each zone, the
inspectors would select a central ginnery and invite all local mechanics
and fitters from the ginneries in that zone to attend a training course in
gin fitting and maintenance; the group would then prepare and set up all the
equipment in the ginnery under the guidance of the inspector. For maximum
impact and in order to ensure continuity of standards, this course should
be offered annually in each zone. During the actual ginning season, the
inspectors should ideally visit each ginnery at least once a month to check
1/   The ideal recruit would be a mechanic with basic training in the rail-
way workshop in Nairobi, supplemented by specialist training in a
ginnery or overseas.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
for faulty-setting and maintenance of the openers, gins, rollers and presses.
Anv faulty equipment could then be stopped and repaired under the inspector's
supervision. The inspectors would also be responsible for running tests of
the Ginning Outturn Percentage (GOP) and for reporting the results to the
Board. These tests should be carried out at least twice per season at each
ginnery. Finally, the inspectors would be charged with ensuring that no B.R.
(second quality) cotton is ginned before AR (first quality) cotton; if this
procedure is not followed much time can be lost in making the necessary adjust-
ments to the rollers and knives ruined by the BR cotton.
12.       Seed Distribution.  One of the most important responsibilities of
the Board is the treatment and distribution of new seed for planting. Parti-
cular attention should be paid to this area if yield increases are desired.
The Board should consider methods of implementing a distribution system to
transport genetically improved seed from the Kibos Experimental Station to
the growers.
Board Financial Controls and Accounting Procedures
13.       Financial Controls.  At present the Board's overhead costs are
consuming about 5%, of its gross receipts from the sale of lint and cotton.
As cotton yields are increased and Board operations streamlined, this per-
centage would be significantly reduced, thus freeing funds for other and
possibly new operations. Specifically, the Board may wish to consider the
establishment with these funds of a price assistance fund for growers; such
a fund could help protect the farmer against sudden down-turns in prices,
and on occasion it might be possible for it to pay a short crop bonus when
circumstances beyond the farmers' control have reduced the level of produc-
tion. If production can be increased in the next several years, and if world
prices continue to gradually advance in accordance with IBRD forecasts, it is
possible that a fund as large as KSh. 4.0 million could be built up over a
period of 4 to 5 years. Any decision to establish such a fund would, however,
require serious study.
14.       Accounting Procedures.  The Board should consider the recruitment
of a fully qualified Chief Accountant, along with an appropriate staff of
accountants invoice clerks and bookkeepers. The Chief Accountant would, in
addition to preparing the annual accounts and financial statements, devise a
system of controls to ensure that proper payments are made for the actual
quantities received (i.e. seed cotton by the ginneries, lint by the buyers,
and seed by the oil mills). The Gross Outturn Percentage (GOP) tests made by
the Inspectors (para 11) would provide a useful guide as to the volume of lint
and seed which should be produced by each ginnery from the seed cotton received
and paid for by the Board. Also, efforts should be made to ensure prompt
payments at all levels to help keep costs to a minimum.
Ownership of Ginneries
15.       In order to ensure that the farmers reap the maximum return from
the cotton industry, Government policy now mandates (para 2) that all privately
owned girneries be bought out by farmers' cooperatives except for a few
ginneries in special areas (e.g., irrigation schemes) which would remain under



ANNEX 7
Page 5
private or statutory board ownership. This change however, should not be read
by the cooperatives to imply that large surpluses will becone available
through these operations for transfer Lo the cooperatives. The Board should
consider means of acquainting the cooperatives with the economics of ginning,
making sure these cooperatives understand that there could be some losses if
commercial management and financial controls are inadequate. For example, the
major source of income for a ginnery in the past has been the overages 1/ in
buying, where the gins received extra quantities for whiently awarded greatly
increased buying commissions, which also contributed to a reduction in ginnery
profits. Thus between these two changes in procedure, the cooperatives are
already receiving the major financial benefits to be obtained from the indus-
try. The remaining surplus is thus unlikely to exceed KSh. 200 per bale.
Prices to Growers
16.       The Board should consider making available to growers its detailed
accounts showing the costs and returns per kilo of seed cotton. These figures
would, as is illustrated below, help to dispell the popular belief that the
Board is making a sizeable profit on its transactions. Best estimates 2/ on
current production levels were used to develop the following figures.
1/   Amounts by which the actual weight of seed cotton exceed approximate
recorded weights on which the payment formulas are based.
2/   Owing to the absence of any accounts since 1971-72 and the suspension
of the chief officers of the Board, figures of current prices and costs
are not readily available, and the figures of costs used above are to
some extent conjectural; they are for the most part based on 1973
estimates without adjustment for inflation. The figures of 37 cents
per kilogram for ginning is the initial cost allowance given to ginners
and is subject to upward adjustment at the end of the season, depending
on the actual costs of the variables in the ginning formula reported
to the Board by individual ginners. It should also be noted that the
prices obtainable for AR and BR seed are considerably lower than the
IBRD forecast for 1975, this is in part due to the fact that the Kenya
Government has imposed price controls on cotton seed oil produced by
the domestic oil millers, thus limiting the price which the latter can
offer the Board for the cotton seed. No cotton seed is exported from
Kenya.

A comparison of the two figures thus indicates a profit of only KSh 0.26/kg
seed cotton. It would seem then that the scope for increasing the return
to the grower is strictly limited unless there is a sizeable increase in
either the world price of cotton or the locally-controlled price of cotton
seed, or a permanent reduction in processing and/or handling costs, none of
which seems likely. Increased productivity through timely planting and
application of insecticides would thus seem to be the most likely avenue for
increased grower incomes. An awareness and understanding of these statistics
on the part of the growers would perhaps facilitate better relations between
the Board and the growers. In the meantime Government has agreed to set



ANNEX 7
Page ;
up the necessary machinery to ensure that a guaranteed minimum price of seed
cotton agreed with the Bank is published annually. For the 1977 season,
Government has agreed to raise the price of AR cotton seed from the K.Sh.2.00
above to K.Sh.2.50 and to adjust prices of other grades in the same proportion.
Lint Price Formula
17.       The Board, in line with a Government request, will begin a study
of possible new formulas by which ginneries could be paid for ginning services.
At the present time the Board is utilizing a revised version of the old formula
applied in neighboring Uganda, under which a ginner receives a standard first
payment for his services (KSh 0.37/kg of lint) plus a second variable payment
determined by his submisson of returns of opening and closing stocks of con-
sumable stores and actual costing of the variables involved. Thus, every gin-
nery would appear to be operating on a cost plus basis, regardless of through-
put and with no real check on variable costs. There is now considerable
support, both within the Board and in Government, for the abandonment of the
old formula and the adoption of a fixed ginning price which would only vary from
one season to another in the light of changing costs. This desire for simplic-
ity is understandable, but it is also true that unless the reimbursements are
based at least in part on costs, there is a risk of over or underpayment with
the latter posing a real threat to the viability of marginal enterprises. The
choice then appears to be between a different price level to every ginnery
based on capital employed in buildings and equipment, variable costs and through-
put (i.e. fixed and variable costs plus a fixed margin of profit) or a uniform
price to all ginners giving differing profit or loss levels and thus an in-
centive to operate to maximum economic efficiencies.
18.       Various alternative thus should be considered by the Board before
making any final decision on this point. Initially, the problem can be simpl-
ified by separating the buying from the ginning function, thus reducing the
level of variable costs. The cooperative societies and the unions might be
given exclusive collection or buying rights for seed cotton, and be paid them-
selves directly by the Board on the basis of weight notes signed jointly by the
society or the union representative and the ginnery representative. This pro-
cedure is already being followed in several areas, and would be relatively
simple to institute elsewhere. This change would then leave the separate
question of remuneration for the ginning and baling operations. Two different
approaches in this regard might be considered by the Board. One solution would
be to divide the ginneries into classes; cost analyses of both the fixed and
variable (staff) overheads could then be made and lump sum first payments
fixed for each class. Other variable costs (e.g. costs of fuel, oil, grease,
lession, twine, baling hoops, studs, etc.) could be determined by reference
to the main suppliers as of a fixed date in advance of the opening of the
ginning season; these costs plus an agreed profit could then be remitted to
the ginners as a second payment on delivery of the cotton bales. A second
solution would be that, assuming that all ginneries are owned by the cooper-
ative societies or unions, the Board could sell the bales and seed and after
a deduction of an agreed percentage to cover the costs of marketing and
management incurred by the Board, pass the proceeds back to the ginneries.
Once the ginnery costs of transport, ginning, and buying were deducted, the
remainder would be available for distribution to the members of the cooper-
atives.



ANNEX 7
Page 8
Terms and Conditions of Sale of Lint and Seed
19.       Sales.  The Board has recently instituted a public auction procedure
whereby all sales of lint and seed by the Board would be published directly
after their completion. This would assure all participants that the sales
are being carried out in a regular and impartial manner. The sales contracts
might also be revised in order to expedite the proceedings, and attention
given to the need for ensuring prompt payment by the buyer. A standard
lint and seed sales contract should be introduced which clearly stipulates
that no party shall be permitted to bid or tender for.seed or cotton lint
unless an adequate irrevocable credit has been established in the name of the
CLStIB prior to the sale. Such a contract should also define clearly the
period within which quality claims would be entertained, and should establish
the arbitration procedure to be adopted in the event of disputes.
20.       Transport.  Some economies would be achieved by the Board if all
lint and seed were sold on an ex-ginnery basis with transport paid by the
buyer. Sales of lint would best be made in 200 bale lots and should prefer-
ably be made before ginning. In this way the buyer could have an agent at
the ginnery to watch the ginning of his purchases and to'check delivery
weights (no weight claims would be admitted thereafter). This procedure
would help to ensure accuracy of weighing and recording, and would allow the
Board to pay the ginneries only for the exact weight recorded, thus reducing
the chance of over-payments. Also, owing to the inaccessibility of the area,
seed cotton from Lamu should be sold on an f.o.b. Mombasa basis.
21.       Quality and Price Controls.  All sales of AR lint cotton are pre-
sumed to be made against a standard FA (first grade) standard box (para 10),
which is renewed annually to ensure against quality erosion. Consideration
might be given to the establishment of at least one other box representing
the UG (undergrade); the differential in cents per pound to be paid for each
could be agreed annually by the Board Classifiers. This procedure could help
greatly in the arbitration of quality claims by providing a more exact standard
of comparison. Recently the Kenya Government has put a limit on the price at
which cotton seed oil may be sold, thus depressing the price tendered by the
oil millers for the cotton seed offered by the Board. With no control on the
latter price, and without detailed knowledge of the standard processing costs,
there is clearly no well-defined policy allocating the benefits of cotton seed
between the grower, the Board, the oil millers, and the consumer of the oil.
The requirement in this Project that Government would review and publish all
commodity prices annually should focus attention on this issue and lead to a
more satisfactory solution.



ANNEX 7
Page 9
Requirements of the Kenya Spinning and lWeaving Industry
22.       Hitherto the volume of cotton lint allocated for sale to local
spinners and for export has been determined by the Board, with the supplement-
ary requirements of the domestic textile industry being met by the purchase
of BR lint from Uganda and Tanzania. However, the recent decline in marketed
production in these two countries has made the local industry more dependent on
local supplies. As these supplies are clearly inadequate to meet the total
potential demand, this might prove to be an auspicious time for the Board to
consider changes in its present methods of allocating the available lint.
Kenya's AR staple coton, 1-1/6" and better, is of a higher quality than re-
quired for much of the local textile production, and thus other things being
equal, it would be more economic to export the Kenya AR lint and import lower
grade and shorter staple cotton from Pakistan and India to supplement the sup-
ply of East African BR cotton. To this end it might be advisable to permit
market forces to decide how much AR cotton should be exported or retained for
local consumption by offering all lint cotton in public auction or tender to
both cotton exporters and local spinners, giving the spinners the option of
exporting their Kenya lint purchases and importing lower grade and shorter
staple cotton to suit their individual requirements.
Future Development of the Cotton Industry
23.       The Board may wish, in conjunction with the other Ministries and
Boards concerned, to consider plans for the future development of the cotton
industry. Particular attention might be given to the changing pattern of
internal demand and prices, and to export price forecasts. Due to competing
claims on the available land for the production of food crops, tobacco, sugar-
cane and sunflower, it is unlikely that there is much scope for a major
expansion in the,area of rainfed cotton. Consequently, any further develop-
ment of cotton production must if desired, be sought through irrigation.
The Board might wish to initiate a comprehensive study of the prospects of
irrigated cotton production in both cotton growing and other areas.

KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICtULTIURAT DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Integrated Agricultural Development
Soils and Climate
Soils
1.        A number of soil surveys have been done for Kenya and most of
the soils of the highland areas have been thoroughly studied and tests made
of the inputs necessary to achieve high crop yields. The regions covered
by the project are characterised as follows:
(1)  Higher altitudes.  The deep red, friable loams where the
rectification of phosphate deficiency leads to good crop
yields. For deeper rooted crops, the available soil
moisture (within the range of field capacity to wilting
point) is usually adequate to help the crop through the
two dry seasons at the beginning and mid-year periods.
However, for annual arable cropping, especially of shallow
rooted crops, the soil moisture availability may often be
critical and this is reflected in the occasional years of
low cereal yields.
(2)  Lower altitudes.  On the plateaus (about 1,300 m) below the
higher elevations, there is a range of soil types, the more
common being that with a lateritic horizon often with
impeded drainage. A second type, also found at these lower
elevations, is the so-called black cotton soil commonly
found in Nyanza, the Kano Plain and some of the coastal
shores of Lake Victoria. These soils present a number of
problems with land preparation and become difficult to
manage under very wet conditions. They have generally
low nutrient status, and available soil moisture is less
than the red soils at the higher elevations. The combi-
nation of climatic vicissitudes and not generally fertile
soils suggests that it is prudent to plan for less than
optimum yield levels at these lower altitudes.
Climate
2.        While the climate of Kenya is mainly determined by altitude,
it is also important to recognize the importance to rainfed agriculture
of the precipitation mechanism. The characteristic bimodal pattern asso-
ciated with equatorial Africa, provides two distinct rainy seasons. These
are somtimes loosely described as the long rains (essentially March through
May) and the short rains (Septerber through November).  Since these are
separated by the two driest months January and July, it is more appropriate
to consider them as the first and second rainy seasons. Fxcept for areas



ANNEX 8
Page 2
close to mountain massifs (such as Mount Kenya), the first season accounts
for about 60 percent of the annual precipitation.
3.        At the higher altitudes average rainfall exceeds 75 mm a month
for as many as 8 of the 121 months and, furthermore, the evapo-transpiration
is appreciably lower than that at lower altitudes and, as a result, perennial
crops such as tea and coffee have been successful. By contrast, for the
lower altitudes (about 4,000 ft) such as Machakos, only 5 of the months have
averages of more than 75 mms and these are interspersed with drier months.
Consequently, perennial crops are rare in these regions and even annual
arable cropping is fraught with varying levels of crop risk.
4.        For the development of an integrated crop and livestock program
for annual arable crops, it is essential to analyse such data as are avail-
able to assist the advisory staff in helping the farmer make the best use
of his given ecological zones. To illustrate the methods, data from two
stations for 16 years were examined in some detail and are shown at Tables
1 and 2. The particular choice of these was determined by the availability
of data rather than specificity to the particular regions covered by this
report.
5.        Data in the top half of Table I show the typical year to year
variation which at first sight seems to be so chaotic as to reveal no well
marked pattern. However, when these same data are arranged in rank order for
months, seasons and years (the lower half of the table), there emerges a
a readily identifiable pattern. Moreover, as the record is of actual events,
it provides a simple statement of probabililty of occurrence. For example,
at p=0.25 (that is an assurance for 3 years out of 4), February rainfall was
in excess of 31 mm, March more than 121, April more than 161, May more than
105 and June more than 45 mm. Were the monthly data statistically independent,
the chances of obtaining such a sequence would indeed be remote. Even for
p=0.50 (the medians) it can be seen that the chances of the 6 month total
at 750.5 mm (the aggregated total of medians) in fact was between p=0.25 and
0.31. Thus the 50 percent level by months provides a useful basis to consider
consumptive use of crops against expected precipitation to estimate likely
risk. Table 2 gives a similar presentation for the rather drier regions of
which eastern Meru, and Machakos may be considered typical.
6.        Table 3 gives some average rainfall data for 9 stations and
indicates not only the wide variation between regions for amount, but
also the extent to which drier months are interspersed, thus adding to
the crop risk.
Analysis to Evaluate Crop Risk
7.        Data shown at Table 4 are set out to illustrate the use to which
the precipitation probability arrangement can be usefully put. In this
particular analysis no open water surface evaporation records were avail-
able in the Ministry of Agriculture librarv, and these should be ascertained
as a matter of urgency. Nevertheless, from comparative localities, these
estimates are probably reasonable representations. Then by using well known
crop factors, it is possible to estimate tlhe separate monthly consumptive



ANNEX 8
Page 3
use for a range of annual crops in such an environment as well as pasture.
Taking a cereal crop which can clearly be planted in March, the 50% expect-
ation and consumptive use would be as follows in m.m.:
Rainfall               Consumptive
expectation                 use
March                 165                      75
April                239                      100
May                   154                     132
June                  70                       84  Deficit 14
July                  54                       60  Deficit 6
These data suggest that for p=0.50, only the months of June and July have
consumptive use in excess of expectation but the amounts of the deficit
are well within the limits of available stored soil moisture (the water
held in the soil profile between field capacity and wilting point). For
the more rigid requirement of p=0.25 (that is 3 years out of 4) there would
be significant deficits from May through July and, were maize the crop in
question, this deficit at silking could have serious effects on yield.
8.        Taking a crop of beans (or groundnuts) it will be seen that at
p=0.50, no great risk is involved since, again, the deficit for December and
January is well within the soil moisture storage.
9.         For cotton, even at p=0.50, there are deficits for 4 of the 6
months so that only rarely would optimum yields be obtained, a factor taken
into account in the later section on crop production estimates.
10.       A similar set of data are presented in Table 5 for Nanyuki and
this shows that if realized values for open water surface evaporation
are of the same order as those here estimated, most crops would suffer
deficits even at p=0.50 much less the more rigid p=0.25   Some indication
of crop risk on a seasonal basis can be obtained by comparing the consump-
tive use requirement against the actual seasonal expectation, thus evading
the difficult problem of separate monthly lack of statistical independence.
11.       For areas of which Kisumu may be typical, the 451 mm requirement
for cereals in the first half of the year has always been exceeded. But
the 464 mm requirement for beans can only be relied on about 50 percent of
the time and for cotton the 538 mm requirement during the second rainy
season can be expected only 3 years in 10 thus committing cotton to
continuous risk.
12.       In the case of the Nanyuki illustrative table, again the cereal
crop requirement is satisfied 75 percent of the time indicating a relative
failure about once in 4 years. Beans also have the even greater risk about
L times in 10 years (and may be a possible explanation as to why Mexican
heans thave not been widely acceptable to small farmers).  By contrast Irish



ANNEX 8
Page 4
Potatoes grown in the first half of the year would seem to be subject to
risk no more often than about once in five years.
13.       An important recommendation which arises from these analyses
is that, as a matter of urgency, analyses of rainfall data for each of the
locations should be subjected to these methods and appropriate crop patterns
and their risk be determined. It is also worth noting that the farmer has a
range of cultural practices which can, to some extent, alleviate the deficit
problem. For example, by widening the spacing (that is decreasing the plant
population) for cotton, the individual plant has a better chance of obtaining
its necessary water reqirement. By contrast for areas where the first rainy
season months are likely to exceed consumptive use, the highest possible plant
population (such as for maize) with associated inputs should be emphasized.
These aspects are further discussed in para. 30 below.
Crop and Livestock Production
Current levels
14.       Data from the MOA show that current crop production from the
14 project districts is derived from about 1 million hectares - mainly of
annual crops, out of a total of about 4 million ha of farm land as follows:

The important conclusions to be drawn from these data are:-
(1)  the dominance of the subsistence crops naize and beans which
together account for more than 90 percent of the annually
cropped area;
(2) the extent to which these 4 provinces contribute to the
National area under these crops;



ANNEX 8
Page 5
(3) that the important cotton crop (worth at present prices
about KSh. 66 million or 1JS$9.3 million annually) is mainly
produced in these Provinces;
(4)   hat of thie estimated 4.1 million ha of f.-:m la,ld reported
to be available, little more than 25 percent is cropped
annually.
With regard to livestock, it has been estimated that about 1.2 million
head of cattle are to be found in the designated Project area. Of these,
about 820,000 are in the Western and Nyanza Provinces where the Zebu type
accounts for 96 percent leaving about 4 percent of grade animals. By
contrast, the cattle population of the Eastern and Central Provinces part of
the project area is about 380,000 head, but here the grade animals con-
tribute 39 percent and reflect the considerable progress which has been
achieved in respect of improvement of smallholder livestock.
15.       According to recent estimates of population and other demographic
data the population of the 14 Districts covered is about 7.9 million with
a land area of 57.2 million, suggesting a land availability of about
7.2 hectares per capita. But such a figure must be taken with some reserve.
In the light of the contrasting environments discussed earlier in this
Annex, it is evident that cotton, for example, and potatoes do not grow
in the same environment. Furthermore the holding size varies considerably
in the climatic areas favoring high population density. Large parts of
the Central Province for example have average holding size of about 2 ha
whereas the less favored areas of the Western and Nyanza tend to have about
6 ha. Tt should also be noted that some holdings are cultivated by more
than one family. With 1.25 families cultivating the average holdings, the
available area per family is thus between 0.6 and 4.8 ha.
16.       For convenience, the Project area has been divided into two
approximately equal zones: the higher and the lower zones. Only maize,
beans and livestock are common to both. But because of holdng size limita-
tions, the livestock management procedures tend to differ. At the higher
elevations, zero grazing is being encouraged whereas the lower elevations
with larger holdings tend to be more extensively managed.
17.       The high zone covers Kisii, Kakamega, Bungoma, Nyeri, Kiambu and
parts of Embu and Machakos. The low zone includes Kisumu, Siaya, South
Nyanza, Busia, Meru, Kirinyaga.
18.       On the basis of 56,000 holdings to be improved and average
area under crops and livestock, the following illustrative patterns
emerge:-

19.       The aggregated project area would be about 71,000 ha in the high
zone and nearly 94,000 ha in the low zone making a total of 165,000 ha which
reflects an average holding size of about 3 ha or 2.4 ha per family.
20.       The smaller annually cropped area of the high zone is often only
part of the source of family income in that this area is suited to coffee,
pyrethrum and tea. The first of these provides a convenient "security" or
"anchor" cash crop against which credit can be obtained. Mention has also
been made of land limitation for providing livestock fodder.
21.       The limitations imposed by the environment on the low zone are that,
with the possible exception of cotton, there is no convenient security crop
against which credit facilities can readily be organized. In view of the
climatic constraints, and the level of inputs necessary to achieve acceptable
average yields, there is understandably some reluctance on the part of the
grower to increase his area under cotton. But at present no equally popular
alternative anchor crop appears to be available even though some farmers grow
sunflower, and groundnuts could also be grown. There does, however, appear
to be a reasonably good prospect for expanding livestock production provided
that disease control and marketing facilities are greatly improved. The
former is of considerable importance at these lower altitudes where a wider
range of debilitating and lethal diseases occur than at the higher zone.
Project Development Proposals
22.       So far as the actual cropped area is concerned, the Project
proposals are based on the following assumptions:



ANNEX 8
Page 7
(i) The first Project year (1976/77) would be a year of
preparation, during which key staff would be hired, and
the adminstrative arrangements for the Project set up.
Thereafter, the 56,000 holdings (70,000 farmers) constituting
about 20% of all the holdings in the Project area would be
brought within the Project at the rate of 20% in 1977-78, 25%
in 1978/79, 25% in 1979/80 and 30% in 1980/81.
(ii) The participating farmers under the Project would be offered
la package under an improved extension service, and improved
credit arrangements for:-
(a) input supplies;
(b) creation of new or improvement of existing marketing
facilities;
(c) seasonal and medium term credit;
(d) training of and demonstration by farmers;
(e) credit or other facilities for land preparation;
(f) cattle health and productivity services.
23.       While it is true that maize yields, using hybrid seed and timely
application of inputs, can easily exceed 30 bags (2.7m ton/ha), it is unlikely
that, even given the closest attention by extension personnel, the time
horizon would be less than 4 years. Consequently, it is considered prudent to
graduate the yield improvement as well as the physical input package. This
means that with an annually increasing number of participating farmers, the
full benefits are not expected until year 8. For the lower altitudes also, an
attempt has been made to allow for a relative crop failure about once in 4
years for cereals. Thus, instead of averaging 2.7m ton/ha as for the higher
elevations, the projected yield at full development is only 2.3m ton. Similar
principles have been employed for other crops and livestock production where
the basic principle of one cow per farmer has been followed. The phasing of
yield levels for the high and low zones is summarized in Annex 14, Table
3.
Project Production Projections
24.       Annual incremental production resulting from the Project is detailed
in Annex 14 for the individual farmer and Annex 16 for the whole Project.
The average net holding income without the project has been estimated by
the appraisal mission to be about KSH 980/ - which corresponds to a family
income of some KSH 784/- (US$97) or US$19 per capita 1/. This compares with
the IBRD estimated poverty level income for Kenya of US$78. The low zone
farmer, with a per holding income of 757/-, and per family income of 606/-
(US$75) or US$15 per capita earns only 19% of the Kenyan poverty level income.
1/ Ignoring non-agricultural incomes and income transfers.



ANNEX 8
Page 8
Even the high zone farmer with a per holding income of 1204/- earns only 963/-
(US$119) or US$24 per capita which is still only 31% of the Kenya poverty
level. At full development the typical low zone farmer would be earning 846/-
(USS105) or US$21 per capita and the typical high zone farmer 1,490/- (US$185)
or US$37 per capita.l/
25.       The cost of inputs on which these Project proposals were based
are set out at Annex 14, Tables 1, 2 and 4. These show that even without
the Project the farm input costs are considerable. In order to achieve the
target yields set out earlier, there will be considerable increases in
these costs, accounting for as much as 67 percent of the Gross Production
Value for the high zone and nearly 77 percent for the low zone. It must
also be emphasised that institutional support, especially for the timely
provision of inputs, is essential for the realisation of the yield levels
discussed in this Annex.
Land Preparation and Mechanisation
26.       Considerable emphasis was laid in the Kenya Government's original
proposals on the shortage of labor for land preparation at periods of peak
demand. In principle, when much of the annual crop production was based
on virtual monoculture on maize or coffee, this contention would be correct.
It is also true that in certain regions, timeliness of sowing can be a very
critical factor in obtaining satisfactory yields. Preparation of a rela-
tively large area by a single farmer would therefore be fruitless under
such conditions. The fact that casual labor is employed suggests an aware-
ness by farmers who would appear to employ each other at appropriate periods.
Also in the context of smallholder agriculture, where the farmers tend to
spread their risks by a series of crop sowings, the bunching of labor peaks
is less evident. For the two zones discussed in this Annex, the high zone
would be expected to have labor demands in March for cereals and potatoes,
and again in September. These would be followed by harvesting support in June
and July and again in January. However, it might well be argued that land
preparation for an aggregate average area of 1 hectare might need some assis-
tance. A survey of some farmers in the field indicated the that this land
preparation could be carried out by hand work of casual labor, were credit
facilities available. This does not mean that for the larger holdings some
form of mechanisation is not desirable but rather that such mechanisation is
not for the small scale farmers for whose benefit this Project is intended.
27.       The input costs shown in Annex 14 reflect the need for casual
labor on the one hand as well as under the category of ploughing (for which
hand cultivation can be substituted). Thus in the case of maize the KSh. 185
for ploughing effectively represents 37 man days per hectare and the separate
category of KSh. 225 a further 45 man days, the remainder being supplied by
the farm family. As and when a real labor shortage develops it would seem
appropriate to consider support for an entrepreneurial contract tractor hire
system which would be financially competitive with costs associated with hand
cultivation.
1/   T noring non-agricultural incomes and income transfers.



ANNEX 8
Page 9
Research
28.       Mention has previously been made of the orientation of research
to large scale agriculture, be it coffee, tea or even maize, for each of
which Research Stations of international repute have long been established
in Kenya. The application of their findings has been successful on this
large scale where availability of inputs through commercially available
credit has not presented serious problems. But the translation of some of
these now well known principles to smallholder agriculture is relatively
recent and the coverage on a very small scale.
29.       In view of the self-evident and urgent need to improve farm
family income, the mission had perforce to make judgements on the minimum
input package to achieve what are shown to be realistic yield levels. As
an interim measure this is satisfactory so long as the complex of insti-
tutional support can be made to function. But there are a number of
problems which must simultaneously be studied during the execution of the
Project.
30.       The first of these concerns a well planned survey of the exist-
ing pattern of agriculture for the two zones, especially taking account
of the farmers' constraints whether real or imagined. An immediate outcome
would be the feed back for experiment stations, rather than research stations,
to provide solutions. It is in this important aspect that the Evaluation
Unit (Annex 9) would play an important role. Furthermore, the practical
demonstration of effective solutions would need to be made at the Farmer
Training Centers (FTC) discussed in Annex 3.
31.       At a somewhat academic level, there is also a need to estimate
the crop water requirements of various crops in relation to rainfall expec-
tation in order to estimate farmers risk in relation to the costs of inputs.
Means of amelioration through, for example, a more imaginative manipulation
of plant populations for specific localities, might well be re-examined.
It is after all, unlikely that a successful statistically correct experiment
with maize in a favorable ecological zone would be equally applicable in
a zone where rainfall expectation is frequently less than consumptive use
by the crop. Even the correct balance between nitrogenous and phosphate
fertilizers under such varied rainfall conditions might well be re-examined,
especially as it is now proposed to have at least one FTC for each of the
districts.
32.       Since cotton is likely to play an important role in the low zone
it is suggested that immediate steps be taken to devise a proper system
of seed multiplication to take advantage of breeding improvements effected,
for example, at Kibos. Starting wit:h the need for between 1200 to 1500 tons
of tested cotton seed with proven germination capacity, this would be derived
from an area producing 1850 to 2300 m tons of seed cotton. In fact, this
is well within the capacity of the Htwea Ginnery. If an isolation area of
say 2500 ha, under irrigation, were available at an average vield of 900
kg per ha. production would be 2250 tons of seed cotton. When ginned, acid
delinted, and dressed with fungicide, this should produce about 1350 tons of
high quality seed. In turn this could be packaged and distributed through
existing channcls of the Kenya Seed Co.



ANNEX 8
Page 10
33.       Planting of the 2500 hectare isolation area at 20 kg per ha would
require 50 tons of seed which, if produced under rainfed conditions at 650
kg/ha seed cotton, would require a separate isolation area of about 100
hectares. Since there are to be farmer training centers for each of the 14
districts, and each of these would have commercially oriented farming, the
approximately 7 serving the cotton based region would need to plant no
more than 15 hectares, each obtaining the seed from Kibos. Attention to
this important requirement is also mentioned in Annex 7 under proposals
for improving the operation of the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Board.

KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Monitoring and Evaluation
1.        There are two main sets of objectives in establishing the monitor-
ing and evaluation system in the Project. The first is to generate reliable
statistical information on some of the basic indicators of the existing
agricultural base and of agricultural performance in subsequent years in
the Project area and to develop the institutional capability required to
carry out this task. Information on yields, cropping patterns and acreages,
is at present inadequate, making it difficult, except in a very general way,
to assess the average base line income, the variability around it and its
relationship to the existing cropping patterns.
2.        Lack of reliable data is partly the result of the limited institu-
tional capability for data collection in Kenya. Due to shortage of trained
staff the Central Bureau of Statistics (CBS) has not collected much agricul-
tural production statistics in the past. Although CBS has begun to expand
the scope of its data collection activities including a socio-economic survey
that is now underway, it is unlikely to be able to cope with the specific
monitoring needs of this Project. It is proposed, therefore, that for the
Project area this function should be carried out in liaison with the CBS
by the evaluation officer in the Land and Farm Management Division, to be
appointed under the Project. Further, it is proposed that the evaluation
officer have the necessary support staff in the form of one assistant at the
head office and one enumerator in each of the districts. The latter would be
responsible for data collection on a fulltime basis in the Project areas in
the districts. The preliminary work related to setting up a data collection
system including defining sampling procedures and pretesting of surveys would
be carried out with the help of a statistical expert to be provided under the
Project on a consultancy basis. In addition, the Project provides for compu-
tational and programming costs of processing and analyzing these data on a
regular basis to assess Project performance.
3.        The second set of objectives in monitoring and evaluation of the
Project would be to assess periodically the managerial effectiveness of the
Project including (a) effectiveness of individual delivery systems such as
extension, credit and input distribution and output marketing; (b) effecti-
veness of coordination among the various services; and (c) the employment,
foreign exchange and other macro-economic effects of the Project. The plan-
ning division of the Ministry of Agriculture, or a unit within CBS, would
have the responsibility to carry out the assessment of these broader benefits
of institutional development expected to accrue under the Project. The
assessment would involve undertaking a number of ad hoc studies periodically
to judge effectiveness of specific interventions. On the basis of such assess-
ments the planning division would make recommendations as to the steps, if
any, that may be necessary for improved implementation of agricultural
serviccrs. This anialysis and the statistics to be collected and analysed by



ANNEX 9
Page 2
the Farm Management Division would be important in the preparation of sub-
sequent phases of the program. It is assumed that the technical assistance
pool from which the manpower would be drawn for reinforcing the planning
division, will provide the necessary skills for evaluation of the Project. It
must be emphasized, however, that the progress on hiring new expatriate and
local staff for the planning division so far has been very limited. Steps
will have to be taken to accelerate this program if manpower is to be avail-
able in time for project monitoring. Alternatively, it has been suggested
that the CBS or a specialist evaluation unit if one is established within the
Ministry of Finance and Planning should carry out this wider evaluation
function as part of an evaluation covering all projects in all sectors of
the economy. Concomittantly, it is recommended that whenever necessary the
staff of the Institute of Development Studies and the University of Nairobi
that have gained experience from the evaluation of the Special Rural Develop-
ment Program be called upon to provide the necessary input in monitoring and
evaluation, particularly since some of the studies would be ad hoc and,
therefore, could be contracted out. These suggestions have been accepted in
principle by the Ministry of Agriculture.

ANNEX 13
Page" 1
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Marketing and Prices
1.        Kenya imports large quantities of certain agricultural products,
such as cotton lint and vegetable oil, because domestic production falls far
short of demand. Furthermore there is likely to remain a strong demand
for such commodities for many years to come. This point is illustrated by
the fact that during 1973 to 1975, the price of sunflower seed more than
doubled and that of castor seed and groundnut nearly doubled. Although Kenya
has been an exporter of maize, pulses, meat, milk and some types of cotton,
due to the high population and income growth the domestic demand for these
cmmodities has also been increasing quite rapidly. Domestic marketing pro-
b;ems have arisen partly from failure in the past to fully exploit scarce
commodities, and partly from certain inefficient licensing, organizational and
other practices of marketing institutions. This is where the major scope
for improvement lies.
2.        At present, farm products are marketed through a series of statutory
boards, national and local cooperatives and private industry. Maize, beans,
oilseeds and many other minor crops are marketed by the Maize and Produce
Board (MPB), cotton by the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Board (CLSMB), milk
by Kenya Cooperative Creameries (KCC), meat by Kenya Meat Corporation (KMC),
passion fruit and potatoes by private traders as well as the Horticultural
Crops Development Authority (HCDA).
Maize and Board
3,        The Maize and Produce Board is a statutory board created by the
"Marketing and Agricultural Produce Act" and the "Maize Produce Act". During
1973/74 it marketed over 38 varieties of produce including maize, several
types of beans, oilseeds, millet and a few other minor crops.- It is vested
with "monopoly" powers for marketing these commodities in the sense that all
movements exceeding 2 bags of maize across district boundaries or exceeding 10
bags within district boundaries must be marketed through an agent of MPB.
Even then, on average it handles less than 15 percent of the total maize
crop.
4.        As of June 1975, MPB had 38 depots for storing grain at various loca-
tions in Kenya and made use of about-3,800 agents for buying and selling the
grain. Nevertheless,.a farmer is allowed to deliver his crop directly to MPB
if he so desires. The margin allowed for the traders is fixed by MPB. Allow-
ance for transportation cost of the grain is included in the margins. The
price paid by MPB to the trader does not vary with seasons or from one geo-
graphical area to another. MPB depots accept maize only if the moisture
content is less than 13 percent.



ANNEX 13
Page 2
5.         Partly due to the increase of price from KSh 50 to 65 in the 1974/
1975 season, the amount of maize delivered to MPB during 1974/1975 was 5 mil-
lion tons, i.e. 1 1/2 million tons higher than the amount in the previous year.
Of the activities being promoted under the Project, prices of maize, meat and
milk are controlled for the portion that is marketed through organized chan-
nels. Prices of other crops depend on market conditions. The Kenyan agri-
cultural marketing system was originally designed to serve large scale farmers.
Its emphasis has changed only slightly in recent years. Marketing of maize
and pulses illustrates this point. Of the 3.7 million bags of maize purchased
by MPB in 1973-1974, 2 million bags were purchased in the Rift Valley Province
alone and another 1.4 million in the Western province. For a variety of rea-
sons, in the past the MPB has not been geared to serving the needs of the sub-
sistence farmer whose marketed surpluses are small, fluctuating and, therefore,
often highly unpredictable. Most such surpluses have been sold through tra-
ditional m.arket channels and consumed in rural areas. However, the effective
functioning of this traditional sector has been inhibi'ted by the marketing and
price policies and by the nature of MPB's operations. As a result, apart from
inadequate extension and untimely input supply, fragmented markets and conse-
quent lack of price incentives have been major bottlenecks to increasing the
productivity and production of the traditional subsistence crops.
6.         Five aspects of MPB policies and organization are particularly
inimical to efficient commodity marketing. First, the MPB is poorly equipped
to assess the size of nearly four-fifths of the maize production in the
traditional sector. The same situation holds in the case of pulses and oil-
seeds. The market intelligence available to MPB needs to be improved not only
to ease MPB's operations but also because a more active role for MPB in this
traditional market is essential to provide the price incentives necessary to
stimulate production in the subsistence sector.  The Project aims to correct
this situation partially by initiating a system of agricultural data collec-
tion in the areas selected for the project.
7.         Second, although the Maize and Produce Board has a number of agents
who purchase produce on behalf of the Board, there are too few of these agents
in some areas to provide a marketing outlet within easy reach of most farmers.
In such cases statutory purchase prices have not been effective floor prices,
particularly because pockets of surpluses have led to temporary decline in
prices in the post harvest period in such surplus areas. The inadequacy of
purchasing agents can, of course, be corrected easily by increasing the number
of agents nominated, since the Board incurs no cost in so doing. The MPB
needs to correct this situation if it is to carry out a more vigorous pur-
chasing policy in smallholder surplus areas in the future.
8.         Third, the small quantities of the total maize and pulses purchased
by the Board, has, partially, been a result of the low support price for
maize until 1975, when the statutory maize price was revised from KSh 50 to
KSh 65 per bag of 90 kgs. Prior to the price revision, given the fragmented



ANNEX 13
Page 3
market structure, in chronically deficit areas it was more profitable for
small'farmers to sell their produce in the open market. In these areas
at times, prices were as much as 100 percent higher than the statutory prices
even in the peak marketing season.  For the Board to be an effective imple-
menting agency of the price policy, it is essential that the prices of sub-
sistence crops be under review on a regular basis and,that they be revised as
appropriate with changing long term market conditions, with particular regard
to relative prices.  Such a pricing policy and reorientation of the scope of
MPB's operations are both crucial if surpluses of maize and other crops
anticipated under the Project are to be realized. Government has agreed to
undertake such price review annually and assurances were obtained during
negotiations regarding that undertaking, as well as setting up the necessary
administrative machinery to formulate and to implement the policy.
9.        Fourth, a related feature of the MPB has been its orientation
to urban (e.g. Naitobi and Mombassa) and export markets. The urban sales
and exports need to be distinguished from equalization of supplies throughout
the country, including those rural areas that are chronically deficit in
cereals. Apart from its desirability on welfare grounds expansion of MPB's
distribution to cover rural areas will allow it more easily than before to
dispose of the stocks acquired through a vigorous program of purchases. To
some extent, the poor crop years of 1972 and 1973-74 and the shortages they
created in areas that are usually self sufficient has already led to a greater
effort in rural distribution by the MPB. The policy of maintaining a strategic
reserve of maize also reflects this change of direction. The Project would
facilitate this action through increase in the storage and drying facilities
to be made available to the MPB in producing areas. These areas are more
easily. accessible to rural distribution points. It would also provide more
staff to MPB to facilitate its purchase and distribution functions.
10.       Fifth, the restrictions on interdistrict movements of maize and
produce on private account exacerbate the problem of regional inequities
and price disparities.. Although licensing of movement is within the juris-
diction of the MPB, partly due to inadequate market intelligence, in the
past the MPB has been unable to facilitate the free flow of surpluses on
private account from surplus to deficit regions. Severe restrictions on
movement of grain have seemed justifiable in the past because there have been
frequent shortages in domestic supplies. With increased surpluses under the
Project, the MPB would have to play a different role, namely one of facili-
tating such interdistrict movements on private account through more liberal
issuance of licenses, so as to provide the necessary price incentive for
production.
11.       In addition to equalizing prices between regions, 'this policy would
also have the desirable effect of leaving the marketing of maize and produce
largely in the traditional sector, which already handles a major portion of
the marketed surplus and which, from all available evidence, incurs marketing
costs that are far smarler than those incurred by the MPB.



ANNEX 13
Page 4
12.       In summary the MPB's more appropriate role should be the one of
stabilizing prices, equalizing distribution and providing a price incentive
as distinct from the one of a major monopoly exporter and the administrator
of movement restrictions, the functions it has been performing over the
years.
13.       In comparison with maize and cotton, the role of statutory Boards
in marketing is expected to be much less important in the case of other
products in the first phase of the project. Nevertheless, even in the case
of these commodities, price policies and development of marketing institutions
will have to receive more attention in the future if Kenya's agricultural
production is to be increased significantly. The discussion below illus-
trates this point.
14.       The Kenya Meat Commission, a statutory body, handles the processing
of ffieat and has monopoly over beef exports at statutory prices. However,
much of the meat produced in the traditional sector does riot find its way
into the organized channel. It is sold in the domestic market through
traditional channels, firstly, because the domestic demand for meat products
has been high and growing rapidly and the prices offered by traditional
butchers have been frequently higher than the statutory prices offered by
the KMC. Secondly, the cattle in the traditional sector often do not meet
export quality requirements. Thirdly, KMC's marketing channels are not
geared to tapping suppliers in the traditional sector. Despite these
limitations of the organized market, because there is unfulfilled domestic
demand and a relatively low rate of growth of the domestic production, the
disposal of the cattle production is not expected to pose any serious
difficulties in this project. For the longer run, the pricing of meat
must, be reviewed in conjunction with that of grains and dairy products.
15.       Crops such as potatoes and milk are also expected to enjoy rising
domestic demand. However, the perishability of these products may pose
marketing problems if marketing channels including, in particular, coopera-
tive organizations do not handle their surpluses effectively. A major
portion of the marketed surplus of potatoes reaches the urban consumer through
the traditional market channels. The studies of th potato market conducted
by the Horticultural Crops Development Authority (HCDA) indicate that' the
private marketing system is reasonably'competitive and that the spread between
the farm level and the urban prices of potatoes is usually small. However,
there are substantial seasonal variations in prices. Further, even a slight
glut in farm level markets leads to a'substantial price decline. If potato
production increases significantly, such seasonal variation may, of course,
be exacerbated. Alternatively the low seasonal prices may inhibit produc-
tion. The cold storage proposed under the Project will alleviate the problem
of seasonal variability somewhat. However, there is also considerable scope
for expanding the role of the cooperatives to include marketing of potatoes.
This will provide an alternative to the private trading channels, thus
increasing competition and reducing seasonal variability.
16.       In the case of milk the Kenya Cooperative Creameries (KCC) already
handles all of the exports and a substantial portion of the milk consumed in
large urban centers. Nevertheless, most of the milk procured by the KCC
comes from the large farmers in the Rift Valley and from the smallholder



ANNEX 13
Page 5
commercial farmers in the Eastern and Central province where dairy cattle has
performed well and milk coopertives are relatively well organized. However,
even in parts of Central Province there are allegations that large quantities
of milk are wasted due to the poorly organized cooperative milk marketing
system; that farmers receive a small fraction of the retail price; that there
is mismanagement of funds, and that the management of cooperative organizations
will benefit from further training and greater insistence on public account-
ability. Milk cooperatives are even less adequate in Westen and Nyanza
provinces. Because milk surpluses have not been significant in this area,
absence of marketing channels has not posed a major problem in the past.
Even after implementation of the Project commences, the initial low volumes
of marketed surpluses are likely to be absorbed in the rural sector through
informal marketing channels. As all Project Cooperatives are expected to be
multi-purpose, they would have to market milk as well as other farm produce.
However, it is essential that marketing prospects for milk and potatoes be
watched continuously and efforts be made to assure necessary facilities suffi-
ciently in advance to avoid marketing bottlenecks. The Project's provisions
for strengthening the cooperative management should also help alleviate the
problem of high market margins and poor price incentives faced by farmers.
17.       Until the development of processing plants in Kenya during the
last two years, edible oil demand was met by imports. Most locally produced
oilseed was exported. More recently some supplies of oilseeds have been
imported from neighboring countries for processing or reexportation. Most
of the institutionalized buying of oilseed has historically been by the
.MPB and the Kenya Farmers Association. More recently, processors have been
buying directly from the farmers. Kenya's crushing industry has traditionally
been restricted to processing cotton seed and copra. With the recent construc-
tion of the factories in Nakuru, processing has extended to include sunflower
and maize germ. In 1975 the total processing capacity was approximately
45,000 tons of seed per annum. Addition{al capacity developed since 1972
totals approximately 20,000 tons of oilseeds and is expandable to 30,000
tons p.a. depending on the availability of raw materials and the demand.
The development of these crops under the project, therefore, assures a supply
for the processing facilities and will help raise their operational efficiency.
18.       The Horticultural Crops Development Authority (HCDA) is the statutory
body that controls the marketing of passion fruit in the overseas markets
through the issuing of export licenses and, in cooperation with the Ministry
of Agriculture, promotes the development of passion fruit growing in addition
to other fruits and vegetables. Passion fruit juice is the major outlet for
this crop and consequently is a growth area for the industry. Most of the
juice is exported, and Kenya Fruit Producers Ltd. (KFP) is the major producer
of concentrate and single strength juice. Domestically, passion fruit is
principally consumed fresh, with juice consumption increasing in recent years
under the influence of the new factory and market promotion by the HCDA who
are undertaking sales campaigns to exploit the market potential, especially
for exports. Passion fruit is exported fresh and as juice. At present Kenya
is one of the major world exporters of these products. However, it has
suffered from lack of a systematic strategy to maintain and expand overseas



ANNEX 13
Page 6
markets. One constraint to increased deliveries of passion fruit by farmers
to processors has been the price paid by KFP compared to the price offered
at the local markets and for fresh exports. In 1974 the KFP price was
33 cents and the KFP has managed to increase the price for 1975 to 45
cents. During the years in which KFP paid low prices, there was a tendency
for passion fruit to be replaced by other more profitable enterprises, and
some growers have been selling all their fruit to the fresh fruit exporters,
thus exaggerating the deficiency in supply to KFP. A further rise to 50
cents per kg sprayed fruit for the 1975 crop is anticipated as part of the
program to realize the target increase in production.
The New Role of Cooperatives in Collection,
Storage and Transportation of Primary Output
19.       The "cotton cooperatives" 1/ have so far been acting as agents
for the Cotton Lint and Seed Marketing Board (CLSMB) in purchasing seed
cotton. With the new development the tasks for the unions would be extended
and they would also have to take on responsibilities for food crops purchas-
ing, storage and transportation. Information about the crop buying capacity
of cooperative societies can generally only be obtained through specific
enquiry at those societies and was in fact obtained with regard to the few
societies visited during appraisal follow-up. The information obtained
leads to the conclusions discussed below.
20.       There seems to be a general lack of transportation capacity in
the unions and probably not enough buying centers. If the unions are to
collect food crops successfully one should aim at a network of centers
within fairly easy reach of the farmers, say 10-15 kilometers.
21.       The cooperatives would continue to buy cotton on behalf of the
Cotton Board and would deliver to the (probably union owned and operated)
ginneries in accordance with present practice. Assurances are being sought
under the project that all farmers in the project area would market seed
cotton through their cooperative societies.
22.       The cooperatives should also be appointed as buying agents for the
Maize and Produce Board for the food crops and would buy and deliver to the
Board at fixed prices. The cooperatives -hould, however, also strive to
establish contracts with processing industry for better prices (groundnuts,
sunflower seed, etc.).
23        The societies and unions would own and operate their own buying
centers and should probably take over those centers owned by the Cotton
Board, as these would now be used not only for cotton but also for other
crops.
1/   Cooperative societies and unions set up specifically to cater for
cotton growers. Under the Project such societies and unions would
handle all crops, as well as milk and meat.



ANNEX 13
Page 7
24.       The cooperatives will have to become more,competitive in their
operations if they are to obtain any share of the marketed food crops.
Private traders usually pay higher than official prices for these crops,
except at the beginning of the selling season, just after harvest. The
cooperatives will probably be able to compete successfully at this time;
but will have to.maintain adequate service (opening hours, etc.) and pay
cash in order to obtain the farmers' confidence. Many instances of neglect
with regard to the service of cooperatives were reported.
25.       It is necessary to create a good buying network if the food crops
are to be made cash crops and there will probably be no need for second
payments as prices for these crops could be fixed at the beginning of the
season.
26.       Also for food crops it is necessary that the new MT accounting
system is operational before cooperatives enter into any more extensive
buying. This system would then facilitate controls in buying and shipments
and losses in transportation and storage will be avoided. These aspects of
cooperative crop matketing are adequately reflected either in the proposed
Work Plans or in assurances Qbtained during negotiations.
