KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTUR. Lt DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Budgets
1.        Tables 1 and 2 of this Annex show the projected cash flow state-
ments for two typical Project farmers, one in the high zone and the other
in the low zone with and without the Project. While these farm budgets
do not represent all the various conditions in the proposed Project area,
they illustrate Project expectations with regard to most of the proposed
beneficiaries. Annex 16, Tables 1 to 5, detail aggregate production farm
costs and benefits, with and without the Project. Tables 3 and 4 of this
Annex show the yield assumptions, input and labor costs on which the farm
budgets and aggregate costs and benefits are based, and Annex 8 describes the
agricultural background to the proposed Project activities.
2.        Yields are particularly difficult to forecast in this Project
because development in each district will vary according to the District
Work Plans, and because of the wide variety of geographical areas and insti-
tutions encompassed by the Project. Yields would be generally greater for
farmers in the higher (usually rainier) zone than for those in the low zone.
Yield estimates are averages taking into account the likelihood of drought
in one year out of four. Yields would thus be higher than these averages
in good years and lower in bad. For the typical farmer, yields are expected
to increase cumulatively until reaching the projected maxima in the fourth
harvest following the farmers' first introduction to Project services. For
the typical high altitude farmer cultivating 2.5 ha, yields are expected to
improve over four years as follows: maize from 1 ton/ha to 2.7 ton/ha;
beans from 0.54 ton/ha to 1.1 ton/ha; potatoes from 5 ton/ha to 18 ton/ha;
passion fruit from 5 ton/ha to 15 ton/ha; milk from 1,100 litres to 1,700
litres per cow, and beef from 100 to 130 kg/ha. For the lowland farmer
typically cultivating about 3.4 ha, yields are expected to increase over
four years as follows: maize from 0.8 ton/ha to 2.3 ton/ha; beans from
0.54 ton/ha to 1.1 ton/ha; cotton from 0.23 ton/ha to 0.65 ton/ha, ground-
nuts (in shell) from 0.60 ton/ha to 1.2 ton/ha; milk from 1,000 litres to
1,600 litres per cow, and beef from 100 to 130 kg/ha. Smallholders in Kenya
have, under similar conditions, with average levels of husbandry, obtained
yields comparable to the above maxima. The four-year build up period
assumed is to enable farmers to become accustomed to the Project's inno-
vations and systems.
Costs
3.        Table 4 shows that, even without the Project, farmers were spending
appreciable sums of money, not only on physical inputs, but also on hired
labour. Furthermore, there was no institutional credit to finance such



ANNEX 14
Page 2
purchases. With the Project, the high zone exenditure per holding on
physical inputs (fertilizers seed, insecticides, cattle feed and hanging
frame for passion fruit) would increase from KSh 476 to KSh 1951 (310%).
This increase would be phased as to 104% in Year 1, 70% in Year 2, and
136% in Year 3. In the low zone, expenditure on physical inputs would be
increased from KSh 482 to KSh 1579 (227%) in stages of 63% in Year 1, 54%
in Year 2 and 110% in Year 4. These stages of increase are designed to
minimize the risk of losses while both the farmer and the institutions are
being introduced to the services, innovations and organizational demands of
the Project.
Labour
4.        At first sight the projections of hired labour with the Project
appear very high at 228 adult man-days (KSh 1140 at KSh 5 per man-day) in
the high zone, and 318 man-days (KSh 1590 - KSh 5 per man-day) in the low
zone. In theory, 1-1/4 families with 1-1/2 adults working a five day week
should be able to provide all the needed labor, since they can work 487
man-days. However, such calculations over smplify the problem by ignoring the
incidence of sharp peaks and deep troughs in the real monthly distribution of
labour demand for the crops and in the areas proposed for the Project. With
land preparation by hand tools as assumed in this Project, the sharpest
labour demand peak would be during land preparation; with lower peaks during
the planting (mainly due to application of fertilizers) and harvesting. On
the other hand, family labour would be considerably under-employed during
the rest of the year, but the seasonality of labour demand peaks makes it
difficult to substitute this family labour for the hired labour. Several
indications support this view, viz. (1) casual labor appears in all farm
budget costings thus showing that farmers either employ each other or that
people are available (in practice, both these factors are at work). (2) Even
without the Project, the average Project holding was already hiring 96 man-days
a year. (3) The farmers indulge in sequential planting, often recognizing
that this does result in some loss of yield but which at the same time spreads
their risk. (4) With the tropical heat, physically exhausting land preparation
with a hand hoe, heavy emphasis on school attendance by children, and social
health and other demands of a typical African rural life, it is widely esti-
mated by agricultural officials in Kenya that the most labour one can expect
from a typical 5-member Kenyan farm family is about 25 man-days a month.
Until such time as the yield improvements begin to show, and that the credit-
worthiness improves to the stage that support would be accorded an entrepre-
neurial tractor hire system, there seems to be limited scope for tractor
ploughing services which would replace the current heavy investment in hired
labour for land preparation.
Benefits
5.        A farm holding of 2.5 ha in the high zone is currently estimated
to provide an income of KSh 1,204 (US$150) and this is expected to increase
to KSh 1,862 (US$231) with the Project. In the low zone the current income
is estimated KSh 757 (US$94) and this is expected to increase to KSh 1,058
(US$131). It must be pointed out, however, that since a holding is on
average owned by 1-1/4 families, the respective incomes per farm family
become:



ANNEX 14
Page 3
High zone    Without the Project  US$120   With the Project   US$185
(US$24 per capita)              (US$37 per capita)
Low zone     Without the Project  US$75    With the Project   US$105
(US$15 per capita)              (US$21 per capita)
Two significant conclusions which emerge from these figures are that, firstly,
with the poverty level in Kenya estimated by IBRD at US$78, the typical
Project farmer in both the high and low zones is at an income level well
below the poverty line. The Project is therefore addressing the needs of the
poorer farmers. Secondly, the income of the typical Project farmer is so far
below the indicated poverty level, that, even with the 40% to 55% increase in
income to be derived from the Project, his income would still be well below
the poverty level. All the above comparisons, however, disregard incomes from
non-agricultural sources, as well as income transfers. This may partly
account for the large gap between Project per capita farm incomes and the
poverty income level.

ANNEX 16
Page 1
KENYA
INTEGRATED AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.        Project incremental costs and benefits are summarized in Table 1.
These are expressed in constant value terms (base = 1975 prices) and are
based on Project cost data in Annex LO and the crop production and input use
data described in Annex 14.
2.        In the calculation of economic costs and benefits foreign exchange
has been shadow-priced at a rate of US$1=KSh 9.30 to reflect the economic cost
of foreign exchange to Kenya. Prices used for maize, groundnuts, cotton and
beef are based on export parity since Kenya is expected to be a net exporter
of these commodities during the Project period. For beans, passion fruit,
potatoes and milk, which are not traded internationally in Kenya, actual farm
gate prices have been used. Yearly prices for farm commodities and fertilizers
have been projected on the basis of the World Bank's commodity price forecast
until 1985/86; thereafter, prices have been assumed to remain at 1985/86 levels.
Input prices other than for fertilizer have been assumed to remain constant at
1975 levels. Commodity prices are shown in Tables 2 and 3, and fertilizer prices
in Table 4.
3.        The Project would increase crop yields per hectare and production
per animal unit, but not the area under cultivation. Project incremental
production estimates are based on realistic yield assumptions (Annex 14).
Farmer participation in the Project would be staggered over a four-year period,
and full yield levels are assumed to be achieved four years after farmers enter
the Project. The calculation of incremental benefits is shown in Table 5.
4.        Project costs include the costs of farm inputs and capital and
operating costs of off-farm infrastructure. On-farm production costs are
based on a yearly input price forecast (para. 2) and do not include interest
charges and any known taxes or subsidies. Input levels have been assumed
to increase gradually over a 3-4 year period. Hired labor, which is expected
to increase as a result of the Project from the considerable traditional
levels, has been priced at market rates. There appears to be sufficient
casual labor available in the Project area to meet Project demands. No
incremental cost has been attributed to farm family labor because the use of
family labour is the same with and without the Project. The calculations are
shown in Table 6.
v.        Capital and operating costs include all costs shown in Annex 10,
Table 1, except costs of training, studies and research, which are not
directly re'lated to the Project; on-farm inputs-and labor, which have been
recalculated (para. 4); and price contingencies. Operating costs after
1981/82 have been assumed to continue at 1981/82 levels. An average annual
amount, based on an assumed life span of eight years for capital items, has
been allowed for replacement from 1981/82 onwards. These costs, which have
bee- a-justed to eliminate taxes, are shown in Table 7.



ANNEX 16
Page 2
6.        The data in Table 1 indicates that the Project would produce an
internal economic rate of return of 37 percent. At the official exchange
rate, the rate of return would be 34 percent. This high rate of return is
largely attributable to the fact that the Project focuses heavily on current
agricultural production and less on infrastructure, with a consequent shorten-
ing of the gestation period for benefits. The Project is confined to esta-
blished areas of cultivation and to farmers who are already familiar with the
proposed farming systems, thus benefiting from a rapid improvement of low
yields with relatively simple packages of modern inputs.
7.        To test the possible effects of various risks, which are always
considerable in a Project aimed at a large number of small farmers who are
unfamiliar with modern techniques of production, a sensitivity analysis was
undertaken. This analysis revealed that the Project's economic rate of return
is highly sensitive to changes in the value of output, cost of onfarm pro-
duction and the rate of increase in farm yields. The rate of return would be
18% if benefits were reduced by 10%; 12% if benefits were delayed by one year;
and 10% if on-farm production costs increased by 20%. If capital and operat-
ing costs increased by 20% and on-farm production costs and benefits remained
unchanged, the rate of return would be 29%. However, a simultaneous increase
of 10% in all costs and decrease of 10% in benefits would result in a zero
rate of return.
